Razi's Responses to the First Proof
Razi provides four responses to the opponents' first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's four critical responses to the opponents' first proof that the non-existent is established.

Core Idea
Razi refutes the proof by reinterpreting key terms and denying the inferences.

Imam al-Razi now responds to the opponents' argument. In four editorial notes, he challenges the notion that distinguishability implies reality, the argument from differential ability, and the consequences of accepting the non-existent as a locus for attributes. His responses show the sophistication of kalam dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read each response
Read the four responses in order. Each responds to a different aspect of the proof.
2
Response 1: Distinguishability and reality
Razi says that the first proof has already been addressed. He reinterprets the commonality of realization in non-existence.
He distinguishes between being stripped of modes vs. being stripped of realization.
3
Response 2: Increase and decrease
Razi notes that increase/decrease entail finitude only in existents, not in non-existents.
This responds to an implicit objection about counting non-existents.
4
Response 3: Composition and agency
Razi argues that making essences existent is an additional act of the agent, like composition; the independence of parts does not imply independence of the whole.
5
Response 4: Attributes and sophistry
Razi rejects that blackness in non-existence is described with multiplicity or that distinction is a necessary concomitant. He concludes that sophistry is not required.
This avoids the charge of sophistry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا بأمرين. الحجة الأولى : المعدوم متميز وكل متميز ثابب فالمعدوم ثابت . بيان الأول من ثلاثة أوجه : أحدها: أن المعدوم معلوم وكل معلوم متميز ، أما إن المعدوم معلوم فلان طلوع الشمس غداً معلوم الآن وهو معدوم ، والحركة التي يمكنني أن أفعلها كالحركة إلى اليمين والشمال والتى لا يمكنني أن أفعلها كالطيران إلى السياء معلومة مع أنها معدومة ، وأما أن المعلوم متميز فلانى أميز بين الحركة التي أقدر عليها والتي لا أقدر عليها وأميز بين طلوع الشمس من مغربها ومن مشرقها وكذلك أحكم على إحدى الحركتين بأنها توجد غدا وعلى الأخرى بأنها لا توجد ، ولا معنى للتميز إلا ذلك ثانيها : أنى قادر على الحركة يمنة ويسرة وغير قادر على خلق السماء والأرض ، وهذا الامتياز حاصل قبل (۱) أقول : أما الحجة الأولى فقد من الكلام فيها . وأما الثانية فإلزام اشتراك الثبوت حالة العدم فهم . معترفون به وقوله « فهي حال فأمر صناعى معراة عن صفة الثبوت، جوابه أنا فرضناها معراة عن الوجوه لا عن النبوت ، ولا يقولون لما به الامتياز ثابتا بل إن كان ولابد فهي أحوال . (۲) أقول : إنهم يقولون الزيادة والنقصان يقتضيان التناهى في الموجودات لا في المعدومات . (۳) أقول : هم يقولون جعل الدوات موصوفة بالوجود أمر زائد عليها كالتركيب الذي هو يدل على الأجزاء وهو بالفاعل ، ولا يلزم من كون الأفراد غنية كون المركب غنيا عنه (٤) أقول : لهم أن يقولوا السواد حالة العدم لا يوصف بالكثرة وأيضاً إن كان معدوداً فالتباين ليس من لوازمها، ولا يجب أن يكون كل ما يكون لازماً للماهية زايلا فلا يكون المعدوم مورداً للصفات المتزايلة والسفسطة غير لازمة .
They argued with two proofs. The first proof: The non-existent is distinguishable, and everything distinguishable is established, so the non-existent is established. The demonstration of the first [premise] is from three aspects: First: that the non-existent is known, and everything known is distinguishable. As for that the non-existent is known, it is because sunrise tomorrow is known now while it is non-existent, and the movement that I can do like movement to the right and left and that which I cannot do like flying to the sky are known while they are non-existent. And as for that the known is distinguishable, it is because I distinguish between the movement I am able to do and that which I am not able to do, and I distinguish between sunrise from its west and from its east, and likewise I judge of one of the two movements that it will exist tomorrow and of the other that it will not exist; there is no meaning of distinguishability except that. Second: that I am able to move right and left and not able to create the heavens and the earth, and this distinction obtains before [existence?]. (1) I say: As for the first proof, the discussion of it has passed [؟]. And as for the second [proof], it is an imposition of the commonality of realization in the state of non-existence; they acknowledge it. And his statement 'they are a state, an artificial matter stripped of the attribute of realization': the answer is that we assume them stripped of the modes (wujūh) not of the realization, and they do not say that that by which distinction occurs is established; rather if it must be, then they are states (aḥwāl). (2) I say: They say that increase and decrease necessitate finitude in existents, not in non-existents. (3) I say: They say that making the essences described by existence is an additional matter upon them, like composition which indicates parts, and it is by the agent, and it does not follow from the individuals being independent that the composite is independent of Him. (4) I say: For them to say that blackness in the state of non-existence is not described with multiplicity, and also if it is counted, then distinction is not among its necessary concomitants, and it is not obligatory that everything that is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity be removed [؟], so the non-existent is not a locus for successive attributes, and sophistry is not necessary.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage contains the opponents' first proof (from two proofs) that the non-existent is established (thābit). The proof has two premises: the non-existent is distinguishable, and everything distinguishable is established. The first premise is supported by three aspects: (1) the non-existent is known (as future events and impossible actions are known), and everything known is distinguishable because we can differentiate between them. (2) We have different abilities regarding non-existent things (e.g., we can move right/left but not fly), and this distinction obtains even before existence. (3) The third aspect is not given in the text. Then Razi provides his responses in four editorial notes. In the first note, he says the first proof has already been discussed, and the second proof (of the opponents? actually the opponent's second proof is not shown) is that the commonality of realization in non-existence is acknowledged; the opponents' claim about 'states' is answered by noting that the essences are assumed stripped of modes, not of realization; and they do not claim that the differentiator is established. The second note: increase/decrease entail finitude only in existents, not in non-existents. Third note: making essences existent is an additional act of the agent, like composition; the independence of the parts does not entail independence of the whole from the agent. Fourth note: blackness in non-existence is not described by multiplicity; even if counted, distinction is not a necessary concomitant; so it does not follow that the non-existent is a locus for attributes, and the charge of sophistry is avoided.

الأحوال
التناهي
التركيب
الصفات المتزايلة
السفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi's responses are merely rejecting the opponents' proof without offering alternative explanations.
Correction: Razi provides nuanced responses that reinterpret key concepts like 'establishment' and 'state', showing how the proof's conclusions do not follow.

Checkpoint
In his first response, what does Razi say the non-existent is stripped of?
A) The attribute of realization
B) The modes (wujūh)
C) All attributes
D) Existence

Key Takeaway
Razi's responses show that the opponents' proof fails because distinguishability does not imply reality, and the concept of 'establishment' is not equivalent to existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that even in abstract theological arguments, precise definitions matter. When you speak about God's knowledge of future events, do you ever conflate 'God knows it will happen' with 'it already exists in some way'? How can you train yourself to avoid such slips in thinking about divine attributes?
?
The opponents' confusion leads to a charge of sophistry, which Razi carefully avoids. In your own intellectual life, where might you be using vague terms that lead to misunderstandings about faith? Identify one area and commit to clarifying your terms this week.
?
Razi's responses show that refuting a false argument is not enough; one must rebuild correct understanding. Is there a false belief about God you have rejected but have not replaced with sound doctrine? What step will you take to fill that gap?

Second and Third Arguments for the Distinction of Possible Non-Existents
Presents second and third arguments for distinction of possible non-existents and conclusion that distinct implies established.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional arguments from Imam Razi proving that possible non-existents are distinct and therefore established.

Core Idea
Desire and action reveal that non-existents are already distinct.

Imam Razi continues his proof that possible non-existents are distinct by offering two more arguments. The second argument builds on the observation that different actions are possible for different agents. The third argument appeals to the common human experience of desiring one thing and disliking another, both non-existent. The passage concludes that distinctness entails establishment (thubut) in the state of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument structure come through.
2
Understand the second argument (entry into existence)
Notice the argument: if non-existents were not distinct, it would be impossible to say that one agent can do an action while another cannot. Ask yourself why that requires pre-existence distinction.
This argument relies on the different possibilities of actions for different agents.
3
Understand the third argument (desire and aversion)
Consider the experience of desiring one non-existent and disliking another. This would be impossible if the non-existents were not distinct before existence.
This is a psychological argument from common human experience.
4
Grasp the conclusion that distinctness implies establishment
The passage argues that distinctness means the essences are determinate and realized. Therefore, possible non-existents are realized in the state of non-existence.
This links distinctness (tamayyuz) to establishment (thubut).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دخول هذه الأشياء فى الوجود ، فلولا تميز بعض هذه المعدومات عن البعض وإلا لاستحال أن يقال إنه يصبح من فعل هذا ولا يصح منى فعل ذلك
•
وثالثها : أن الواحد منا قد يريد شيئاً ويكره شيئاً آخر ، وإن كان المراد والمكروه بعد معدومين ولولا امتياز المراد عن المكروه قبل الوجود وإلا لاستحال أن يكون أحدهما مراداً والآخر مكروها ، فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة أن المعدومات الممكنة متميزة وأما أن كل متميز ثابت فلانا لا نعنى بالثابت إلا كون هذه الماهيات في أنفسها متعينة ومتحققة ، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن امتياز هذه الماهية عن تلك الماهية لا يحصل إلا بعد تحقق هذه الماهية وتحقق تلك الأخرى ، فعلمنا أن هذه الماهيات متحققة حال العدم.
The entry of these things into existence, for if there were no distinction between some of these non-existents and others, it would be impossible to say that it becomes possible to do this and not possible for me to do that. And third: that one of us may desire something and dislike another thing, even though the desired and disliked are both non-existent; and if there were no distinction between the desired and the disliked before existence, it would be impossible for one to be desired and the other disliked. Thus it is established by these three aspects that possible non-existents are distinct. And as for that every distinct thing is established, it is because we do not mean by 'established' except that these essences in themselves are determinate and realized. And it is known by necessity that the distinction of this essence from that essence does not occur except after the realization of this essence and the realization of that other. So we know that these essences are realized in the state of non-existence.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a proof that possible non-existents (ma'dumat mumkina) are distinct (mutamayyiza) and therefore established (thabita) in non-existence. It gives two of the three arguments (the first being on the previous page). The second argument: if things entering existence were not distinct prior to existence, it would be impossible to say that one act is possible for an agent and another is not. The third argument: a person can desire one non-existent and dislike another; this requires that the desired and disliked are distinct before existence. The conclusion: all possible non-existents are distinct. Then the argument proceeds: every distinct thing is established, because 'established' means the essence is determinate (muta'ayyina) and realized (mutahaqqiqa). Distinction between two essences requires that each be realized. Hence these essences are realized in the state of non-existence.

Key Terms
ma'dum mumkin
tamayyuz
thabit
muta'ayyin
mutahaqqiq

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental distinction does not require any reality outside the mind.
Correction: The argument claims that distinction entails extra-mental reality (thubut) in the state of non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the third argument given in this passage for the distinction of possible non-existents?
A) The argument from the entry of things into existence
B) The argument from desire and aversion
C) The argument from the impossibility of infinite regress
D) The argument from the necessity of possibility

Key Takeaway
Possible non-existents are distinct and therefore established, as shown by the different possibilities of action and the experience of desire and aversion.

Second Proof: The Distinction of the Possible from the Impossible
Proof that the possible non-existent is established via opposition with impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi argues that the possible non-existent is established by analyzing the nature of possibility and impossibility as opposites.

Core Idea
Possibility must be real because impossibility cannot be.

Imam Razi presents a concise logical proof that the possible non-existent has a kind of reality. He argues that if impossibility were a positive attribute, the impossible would be necessary—a contradiction. Therefore impossibility is privative, and since opposites require one positive, possibility must be real. This establishes that possible things have a distinct reality even before existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: it starts with a claim, argues by contradiction, and concludes.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'wasf thubuti' and 'wasf salbi'.
2
Identify the key logical steps
Break down the proof into three parts: (1) Impossibility cannot be a positive attribute; (2) If it were, the impossible would be necessary; (3) Therefore, since opposites need one positive, possibility is positive.
Focus on the reasoning about opposites (al-mutaqabilan).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: What does it mean for the possible non-existent to be 'established'? The proof shows that possibility is a real attribute, so the possible thing has a distinct reality even before existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن المعدوم الممكن متميز من المتنع ولا يجوز أن يكون الامتناع وصفا ثبوتيا وإلا لكان الموصوف به ثابتا فيكون الممتنع الثبوت واجب الثبوت هذا خلف ، وإذا لم يكن الامتناع ثبوتيا كان الإمكان ثبوتيا ضرورة ؛ لأنه لابد فى المتناقضين من كون أحدهما ثبوتيا والآخر سلبيا ، والموصوف بالوصف الثبوتى ثابت فالمعدوم الممكن ثابت .
The second proof: that the possible non-existent is distinct from the impossible, and it is not permissible for impossibility to be a positive attribute (wasf thubuti), otherwise the thing described by it would be established, and then the impossible-of-existence would be necessary-of-existence—a contradiction. And when impossibility is not positive, then possibility must be positive of necessity, because it is necessary in the case of the two opposites that one of them be positive and the other privative. And what is described by a positive attribute is established, so the possible non-existent is established.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-hujja al-thaniya) that the possible non-existent (ma'dum mumkin) is established (thabit). It argues from the contrast between possibility and impossibility. Impossibility (imtina') cannot be a positive attribute (wasf thubuti), because if it were, the subject (the impossible thing) would be established, making the impossible thing necessary—a contradiction. Therefore impossibility is privative (salbi). Since possibility and impossibility are opposites (mutaqabilan), one must be positive and the other privative. Thus possibility is positive. Anything described by a positive attribute is established. Hence the possible non-existent is established. The proof relies on the metaphysical principle that opposites cannot both be privative; one must be real.

Key Terms
al-mumtani'
wasf thubuti
wasf salbi
al-mutaqabilan

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is merely a mental consideration with no real basis.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that possibility is a real positive attribute because its opposite (impossibility) cannot be positive, and opposites require one to be real.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why must possibility be a positive attribute?
A) Because impossibility is a positive attribute
B) Because possibility and impossibility are opposites, and one must be positive
C) Because the possible non-existent is not distinct from the impossible
D) Because possibility is a mental construct

Key Takeaway
The possible non-existent is established because possibility is a real positive attribute, as shown by the logical relationship between possibility and impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often speak of things as 'impossible' for you—whether changing a habit, forgiving someone, or drawing closer to Allah. Based on this proof, is that 'impossibility' a real attribute of your soul or a privation you have accepted? What would it mean to treat it as a privation that can be removed?
?
The proof establishes that possibility is a real, positive attribute grounding existence. Consider a spiritual goal you have relegated to 'possible in theory but not for me.' If possibility is real, what does your reluctance reveal about your belief in your own potential? Are you denying a real attribute that Allah has placed in you?
?
This argument uses logical necessity to affirm the reality of possibility. When you contemplate your own existence as a possible being contingent on Allah, does that lead you to greater gratitude and reliance on Him? Or do you take your existence for granted, as if you were necessary? How would your life change if you truly felt your possibility at every moment?

Four Counterexamples Against Extra-Mental Reality
Razi lists four counterexamples to show that mental distinction does not necessitate extra-mental reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four specific counterexamples that demonstrate that mental distinction (tamayyuz dhihni) does not entail extra-mental reality (thubut khariji).

Core Idea
Mental distinctness alone does not prove external existence—four examples show this.

This passage is part of a debate about whether non-existent things can be said to be established. The author rejects the premise that every distinct non-existent is established and instead offers four counterexamples. Each counterexample illustrates a case where we can mentally distinguish something that clearly has no extra-mental reality. Understanding these arguments clarifies the relationship between mental concepts and external existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: the author begins by denying the claim, then lists four objections with 'first', 'second', etc., and ends with a conclusion.
The passage is a direct response to a previous argument, so it assumes you know the claim being refuted.
2
Identify the four objections
Read each objection one by one. For each, ask: what mental distinction is being made, and why does it not imply extra-mental reality? Write down the key point of each.
The objections involve: (1) judging a partner to God impossible, (2) imagining fictional objects, (3) distinguishing existences of motions before they exist, (4) understanding composition or states like motionness.
3
Understand the conclusion
After the four objections, the author states his conclusion: mental distinction does not necessitate extra-mental reality. Reflect on how each objection supports this.
The conclusion ties all four examples together as evidence against the original claim.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you can identify which example does not belong to the four. This reinforces the material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول: لا نسلم أن كل معدوم ثابت ، والذى احتجوا عليه فهو معارض بأمور أربعة : أولها : أنا حكم على شريك الله تعالى بالامتناع ، ولولا أنا نتصوره متميزا عما عداه لاستحال الح الحكم عليه بالامتناع ، لان مالا يتصور لا يمكن الحكم عليه .
وثانيها : أنا نتصور بحراً من زئبق وجبلا من ياقوت ونحكم بامتياز بعض هذه المخيلات عن بعض مع أنها غير ثابتة فى العدم؛ لأن الجبل من الياقوت عبارة عن أجسام قامت بها أعراض وعندكم ماهيات الجواهر والأعراض وإن كانت ثابتة فى العدم لكن الجواهر غير موصوفة بالأعراض حال العدم فلا يمكن تقرير ماهية الجبل من حيث إنه جبل حال العدم.
•
وثالثها : أنا نتصور وجودات هذه الماهيات قبل دخولها في الوجود ونحكم بامتياز بعض تلك الوجودات من بعض فإني كما أعقل امتياز ماهية الحركة امنة من ماهية الحركة يسرة قبل دخولهما في الوجود كذلك أعقل امتياز وجود إحدى الحركتين عن وجود الأخرى قبل دخولهما في الوجود ، فلو اقتضى العلم بامتياز الماهيات تحققها في العدم لاقتضى العلم بامتياز هذه الوجودات تحققها فى العدم وذلك باطل بالاتفاق ، ولان الوجود مناقض للعدم والجمع بينهما محال ورابعها : أنا تعقل ماهية التركيب والتأليف قبل دخولها في الوجود وهذه الماهية يمتنع تقريرها في العدم ؛ : لأن التأليف عبارة عن اجتماع الأجزاء وتماسها على وجه مخصوص ، وذلك يمتنع تقريره حال العدم بالاتفاق وإذا كان كذلك استحل أن يتقرر ماهية التأليف حال العدم، ثم إنا نتصورها قبل وجودها وتميز بينها وبين ساز الماهيات ، وكذلك نعقل المتحركية والساكنية قبل حصولهما مع أنهما من قبيل الاحوال ولا حصول لهما فى العدم، فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن التميز الذهى لا يستدعى تحقق الماهيات خارج الذهن
And the response to the first [proof]: we do not concede that every non-existent is established. What they argued for is countered by four matters. First: we judge that a partner to God, exalted, is impossible; and if we did not imagine it distinct from other things, it would be impossible to judge it as impossible, because what is not imaginable cannot be judged. Second: we imagine a sea of mercury and a mountain of ruby and we judge that some of these imaginings are distinct from others, even though they are not established in non-existence; because the mountain of ruby is an expression of bodies that have accidents, and according to you the essences of substances and accidents, although they are established in non-existence, yet substances are not described by accidents in the state of non-existence, so the essence of the mountain as a mountain cannot be realized in the state of non-existence. Third: we imagine the existences of these essences before their entry into existence and we judge that some of those existences are distinct from others; for just as I understand the distinction of the essence of motion to the right from the essence of motion to the left before their entry into existence, likewise I understand the distinction of the existence of one motion from the existence of the other before their entry into existence. So if knowledge of the distinction of essences necessitated their realization in non-existence, then knowledge of the distinction of these existences would necessitate their realization in non-existence—and that is false by consensus, because existence contradicts non-existence and their combination is impossible. Fourth: we understand the essence of composition and arrangement before its entry into existence, while this essence cannot be realized in non-existence, because arrangement is an expression of the joining and contact of parts in a particular manner, and that is impossible to realize in the state of non-existence by consensus. If this is so, then it is impossible that the essence of arrangement be realized in the state of non-existence. Yet we imagine it before its existence and distinguish it from other essences. Likewise, we understand motionness and stillness before they occur, even though they are of the category of states (ahwal) and have no occurrence in non-existence. Thus it is established by these aspects that mental distinction does not necessitate the realization of essences outside the mind.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is the response (jawab) to the first proof that claimed every distinct non-existent is established. The author denies the premise (la nusallim) and presents four counterexamples (mu'arada bi-umur arba'a). First: we can judge the impossibility of a partner to God; this judgment requires imagining that partner as distinct, yet the partner is not established in actuality. Second: we imagine a sea of mercury and a mountain of ruby—clearly fictional—and distinguish them, yet they have no extra-mental reality; even if one grants that the essences of substances and accidents are established in non-existence, the specific composition (e.g., a mountain) cannot be realized because accidents do not inhere in substances in non-existence. Third: we can imagine the existences of essences before they exist and distinguish them; if mere mental distinction implied extra-mental reality, then the existences (which are mental concepts) would also be real in non-existence, which is absurd since existence and non-existence are opposites. Fourth: we understand the essence of composition (tarkib) before existence, but composition cannot be realized in non-existence because it requires parts actually joining. Similarly, we understand 'motionness' and 'stillness' as states (ahwal) that have no existence in non-existence. The conclusion: mental distinction (al-tamayyuz al-dhihni) does not entail extra-mental reality (tahaqquq al-mahiyyat kharij al-dhihn).

Key Terms
tamayyuz dhihni
thubut khariji
mumtani'
mukhayyalat
ahwal

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we can distinguish two non-existent things in the mind, they must have some reality outside the mind.
Correction: Mental distinction is possible without external reality, as shown by these four examples: judging a partner to God, imagining fictions, distinguishing existences of motions before they exist, and understanding composition and states.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four counterexamples given against the claim that every distinct non-existent is established?
A) Judging a partner to God as impossible
B) Imagining a sea of mercury and a mountain of ruby
C) The distinction between the existence of two motions before they exist
D) The distinction between a necessary and a possible existent

Key Takeaway
Mental distinction (tamayyuz dhihni) is a conceptual tool, not proof of external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine something intensely, do you sometimes act as if it were real? How might that lead you to wrong beliefs about God? Are you willing to examine your imagination and submit it to revelation?
?
This lesson distinguishes between mental concepts and extra-mental reality. Is there a belief you hold strongly that might be only a mental construct, not grounded in truth? Are you ready to test it against the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
The first counterexample shows that we can judge something as impossible without it being real. What false ideas about God's attributes might you be rejecting only because you can't imagine them? Are you open to what Allah has revealed beyond your imagination?

The Opponents' Proofs for Thubut of Non-Existent
Author summarizes two proofs for thubut and gives the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main proofs opponents use to argue that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) has thubut (established reality), and the author's initial response.

Core Idea
Mental distinction does not necessarily imply extra-mental reality.

In this passage, the author summarizes the key arguments of those who claim that non-existent entities have a kind of stable reality (thubut). He presents their first proof from distinction in knowledge, and their second proof from the symmetry of possibility and impossibility. He then outlines the standard response: that the distinctions are mental and do not require external existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the first proof (from distinction in knowledge/power/will) and the second proof (from possibility vs. impossibility). Notice how each is structured.
The second proof is deemed unsatisfactory by the opponents themselves.
3
Examine the response
The author counters by listing mental distinctions (impossibles, possibles, states) that opponents acknowledge but do not consider extra-mental. Understand why this works as a counter.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why mental distinction alone is insufficient to prove external thubut. How does this apply to the broader debate?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : حاصل ما أورده من حججهم على أن المعدوم ثابت هو استدلالهم في الحجة الأولى بالتميز على الثبوت وإثبات التميز فى العلم والمقدرة والإرادة حال كون المعلومات والمقدورات والمرادات معدومة ، وادعاء أن النميز يقتضى الثبوت بالضرورة .
وفي الحجة الثانية : بأن الإمكان مقابل الامتناع والامتناع غير ثابت فمقابله ثابت وهذه الحجة ليست مرضية عندهم فإنهم لا يقولون بثبوت الإمكان والامتناع ومقابلهما وحاصل الجواب المعارضة بإثبات التميز في الممتنعات والممكنات والأحوال كا وجود والتركيب والمتحركية والساكنية وهم لا يقولون بثبوتها ، ثم ذكر أن هذه التميزات ذهنية وهى لا تستدعى ثبوت خارجيا .
(١) أقول : هذا تأكيد للمعارضة وبيار عدم الفرق بين ما يقر به ويدعى ثبوته وبين مالا يقر به من المعدومات المتميزة في الذهن
•
(٢) أقول : إنه يقول أثر القدرة والإرادة في المعدوم الثابت هو جعله موصوفا بالوجود الذي هو وراء الثبوت، وأنت ما أبطلت ذلك .
أمر
فإن قلت إنى أعلم أن الوجود هو الثبوت بالبديهة ، فلم لم تقل في أول الباب إن دعواكم بأن المعدوم شيء باطل بالبديهة ويستريح من هذا التطويل .
(۳) أقول : قد مر أنهم لا يقولون بذلك ، واو فالوا المكان الهم أن يقولوا إمكان الثابت في العدم جواز اتصافه بالوجود بعد العدم، ولا لزم من ذلك خروجه عن الدانية بل يتغير من حيث يحصل له صفة بعد أن لم يكن، وأيضاً لا يلزم من حمل المنفى على الممتنع حمل الثابت على الممكن ، والالكان كل ممكن ثابتا بل موجوداً .
(1) I say: The sum of what he [the opponent] presented from their proofs that the non-existent is established is their using distinction in the first proof to establish thubut, and affirming distinction in knowledge, power, and will while the known, objects of power, and objects of will are non-existent, and claiming that distinction necessitates thubut necessarily. In the second proof: that possibility is opposite to impossibility, and impossibility is not established, so its opposite is established—but this proof is not satisfactory to them, because they do not affirm the thubut of possibility and impossibility and their opposites. The sum of the response is the counter-argument by affirming distinction in impossibles, possibles, and states such as existence, composition, motionness, and stillness—and they do not affirm their thubut. Then he mentioned that these distinctions are mental and do not necessitate extra-mental thubut.
(1) I say: This is a reinforcement of the counter-argument and a clarification of the lack of difference between what they acknowledge and claim to be established and what they do not acknowledge of non-existents that are distinct in the mind.
•
(2) I say: He [the opponent] says that the effect of power and will on the non-existent established thing is to make it described by existence, which is beyond thubut, and you [the author] have not refuted that.
As for your saying: I know that existence is thubut by intuition, why did you not say at the beginning of the chapter that your claim that the non-existent is a thing is false by intuition, and we would be relieved of this lengthy discussion?
(3) I say: It has passed that they do not say that, and if they said that place? [unclear] they could say: the possibility of the established in non-existence is the permissibility of its being described by existence after non-existence, and that does not entail its departure from essence-ness, but rather it changes in that it acquires an attribute after not having it. Also, it does not follow from applying negation to the impossible to apply thubut to the possible; otherwise every possible would be established, indeed existent.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage consists of the author's own marginal comments (aqul) summarizing and critiquing the arguments. The first note summarizes the opponents' first proof: they argue that distinction in knowledge, power, and will while the objects are non-existent proves that distinction implies thubut. The second proof (from possibility vs. impossibility) is also summarized but deemed unsatisfactory because the opponents do not accept thubut for possibility and impossibility. The response is to counter by listing mental distinctions (impossibles, possibles, relations) that the opponents acknowledge but do not consider established externally. The second note reinforces that there is no difference between the cases the opponents accept and those they reject: all are mental distinctions. The third note responds to a potential objection: the opponent might claim that power and will affect the non-existent by adding existence (which is beyond thubut). The author then says: if you object that existence is thubut by intuition, why not just say their claim is false by intuition? The final note addresses the nature of possibility: if the opponent says possibility is the permission of acquiring existence after non-existence, that does not entail change in essence, and one cannot infer thubut from possibility; otherwise every possible would be existent.

Key Terms
thubut
tamayyuz
qudra
irada
imkan
imtina'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any mental distinction proves extra-mental reality.
Correction: The author shows that mental distinctions can exist without external thubut, as with impossibles and states.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first proof for thubut?
A) The symmetry of possibility and impossibility
B) Distinction in knowledge, power, and will while objects are non-existent
C) The existence of mental concepts
D) The impossibility of change in essence

Key Takeaway
The opponents' proofs for thubut rely on mental distinction and modal opposition, but the author shows these are not sufficient to prove extra-mental reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often mentally distinguish between your own intentions and those of others, assuming you know their inner states. How does this passage challenge your assumption that mental distinctions correspond to objective reality? Will you commit to verifying your judgments against the outward evidence before acting on them?
?
When you learn about unseen matters like the Afterlife, do you treat your mental images as if they were the reality itself? This lesson warns that mental distinctions do not imply external existence. How can you ensure that your belief in the ghayb is based on revelation and not on your own mental constructs?
?
Reflect on a time you argued for something because it seemed 'logically necessary' in your mind. Did you confuse mental certainty with actual truth? Are you willing to examine your own reasoning and accept that internal conviction does not always match external reality?

Clarifying Existence, Thubut, and Essence
Author responds to objections about existence as additional attribute and clarifies relation between possibility and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's further clarifications on the relationship between existence and thubut, and the objection that existence is additional to thubut.

Core Idea
Existence is not beyond thubut; mental distinction does not change essence.

In the second and third marginal notes, the author anticipates a potential objection: that the opponent might claim that existence is an additional attribute beyond thubut. He also responds to the idea that if existence were identical to thubut, the opponent's position would be refuted by intuition. Finally, he clarifies that possibility does not entail a change in essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the two notes
Read the Arabic and translation for both the second and third marginal notes. Notice the flow from objection to response.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the opponent claims that existence is 'beyond thubut'. Understand the logic: power and will act on the non-existent by adding existence.
3
Analyze the author's reply
The author points out that if existence were identical to thubut, the opponent's claim would be false by intuition. Then he clarifies that possibility does not change essence.
The third note uses an example: applying negation does not make something possible.
4
Consider the final point
The author states that possibility does not imply thubut; otherwise every possible would be existent. Reflect on why this matters.

Refuting the Argument from Knowledge
Razi presents a dialectical strategy to refute the argument from knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to refute the claim that the non-existent is 'known' by analyzing what 'known' means.

Core Idea
Being known does not prove extra-mental existence.

Imam al-Razi teaches us a powerful dialectical move: when an opponent uses a vague term, ask for its precise meaning. Here, the opponent claims the non-existent is 'known' (ma'lum). By clarifying two possible meanings, we can neutralize the argument without accepting any extra-mental reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the translation.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'known'
The author presents two possible interpretations of what 'known' might mean.
One meaning is conceded but irrelevant.
3
Understand the response
The author shows that neither interpretation proves extra-mental establishment.
4
Reflect on tightening the debate
Consider how asking for definitions can strengthen your position in theological discussions.
This is a key dialectical technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنك إن أردت تضييق الكلام على الخصم فقل ، ما الذى تعنى بكون المعدوم معلوما إن عنيت به ذلك الضرب من الامتياز الذي تجده في تصور المتنعات والمركبات والإضافيات وذلك مسلم ، لكنه لا يقتضى تقرر الماهيات في العدم بالاتفاق ، وإن عنيت به أمر اوراء ذلك فلا بد من إفادة تصوره ثم إقامة الحجة عليه فإننا من وراء المنع في المقامين
•
وأما قوله: المعدوم مقدور والمقدور متميز فضعيف لأن المقدور إما أن يكون ثابتا في العدم أو لا يكون ، فإن كان ثابتا لم يكن للقدرة فيه تأثير البتة لأن إثبات الثابت محال ، وإذا كان كذلك استحال أن يكون مقدورا، وإن لم يكن ثابتا كان ذلك اعترافا بأن المقدور غير ثابت وحينئذ لا يمكنهم الاستدلال بكونه مقدورا على كونه ثابتا ، وهذا هو الجواب عن قولهم المعدوم مراد وكل مراد ثابت
•
والجواب عن الحجة الثالثة : أن المحكوم عليه بكونه ممكنا إما أن يكون ثابتا في العدم أو لا يكون . والأول باطل لأن عندكم الذوات المعدومة يمتنع عليها التغير والخروج عن الذاتية فلا يمكن جعل الإمكان صفة لها ، وإن كان الثاني كان الإمكان وصفه لما ليس بثابت فى العدم وحينئد لا يمكنكم الاستدلال بالإمكان على كون الممكن ثابتا في العدم ـ وبالله التوفيق
Then, if you wish to tighten the discussion against the opponent, say: what do you mean by the non-existent being 'known'? If you mean by it that kind of distinction which you find in the conception of impossibilities, composites, and relatives, then that is conceded, but it does not necessitate the realization of essences in non-existence by consensus. If you mean by it something beyond that, then it is necessary to convey its conception and then establish proof for it, for we are in the position of denial in both cases.
•
As for his statement: 'the non-existent is an object of power (maqdur) and the object of power is distinct', it is weak, because the object of power is either established in non-existence or not. If it is established, then power has no effect in it at all, because making something established is impossible. If that is so, it is impossible for it to be an object of power. If it is not established, then that is an admission that the object of power is not established, and then they cannot use its being an object of power to prove that it is established. This is the response to their statement 'the non-existent is an object of will (murad) and every object of will is established.'
•
And the response to the third proof: that the thing judged to be possible (mumkin) is either established in non-existence or not. The first is false because, according to you, the non-existent essences cannot undergo change or departure from essence-ness, so possibility cannot be made an attribute for them. If the second [is the case], then possibility is an attribute of what is not established in non-existence, and then you cannot use possibility to prove that the possible is established in non-existence. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage provides three further rhetorical moves. First, the author advises tightening the argument by asking the opponent what they mean by 'known' (ma'lum). If they mean mental distinction, it is granted but does not prove extra-mental reality. If they mean something else, they must define and prove it; the author withholds assent. Second, the argument that the non-existent is an object of power (maqdur) and therefore distinct is refuted: if the object of power is already established, power has no effect; if not, then the opponent admits it is not established. The same response applies to will (irada). Third, the response to the third proof (that possibility implies establishment): if the possible is established, then possibility cannot be a new attribute because essences do not change; if not established, then possibility predicates something not established, so the argument fails. The passage ends with 'wa billahi al-tawfiq'.

Key Terms
ma'lum

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is known, it must exist in some way outside the mind.
Correction: Being known can be a purely mental attribute that does not require any extra-mental reality, as shown by the fact that impossibilities and composites are known yet have no external existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first possible meaning of 'known' (ma'lum) that the opponent might intend?
A) Extra-mental existence
B) The kind of distinction found in conceiving impossibilities
C) Being an object of power
D) Being established in non-existence

Key Takeaway
The term 'known' can refer to a mental attribute that does not entail extra-mental existence, so the argument from knowledge fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you assumed that because you could conceive of something clearly, it must exist. How does al-Razi's argument challenge that assumption? What will you do differently now?
?
In your study of aqeedah, you have learned about many Unseen realities. How does this lesson help you distinguish between mental concepts that are true and those that are merely imagined? Identify one such distinction you must make in your own belief.
?
Consider a doubt that has crossed your mind about a matter of faith. Could it be that your doubt arises from confusing mental conception with real existence? Write down how you will use this lesson to respond to such doubts.

Refuting the Arguments from Power and Will
Razi refutes the proof from power and will with a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the claim that being an object of power or will implies extra-mental establishment.

Core Idea
Being an object of power does not prove reality.

The opponent argues that the non-existent is an object of power (maqdur) and will (murad), and therefore must be distinct and established. Imam al-Razi responds with a dilemma: if the object is already established, power has no effect; if not, then the opponent admits it is not established. The same logic applies to will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the argument.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Notice that the opponent says the non-existent is an object of power and will, implying it is distinct and established.
3
Understand the dilemma
The author presents two cases: whether the object is established or not. Each leads to a problem for the opponent.
4
Apply the same response to will
The same dilemma refutes the argument from will as well.
5
Conclude the argument fails
Realize that being an object of power or will does not prove extra-mental establishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنك إن أردت تضييق الكلام على الخصم فقل ، ما الذى تعنى بكون المعدوم معلوما إن عنيت به ذلك الضرب من الامتياز الذي تجده في تصور المتنعات والمركبات والإضافيات وذلك مسلم ، لكنه لا يقتضى تقرر الماهيات في العدم بالاتفاق ، وإن عنيت به أمر اوراء ذلك فلا بد من إفادة تصوره ثم إقامة الحجة عليه فإننا من وراء المنع في المقامين
•
وأما قوله: المعدوم مقدور والمقدور متميز فضعيف لأن المقدور إما أن يكون ثابتا في العدم أو لا يكون ، فإن كان ثابتا لم يكن للقدرة فيه تأثير البتة لأن إثبات الثابت محال ، وإذا كان كذلك استحال أن يكون مقدورا، وإن لم يكن ثابتا كان ذلك اعترافا بأن المقدور غير ثابت وحينئذ لا يمكنهم الاستدلال بكونه مقدورا على كونه ثابتا ، وهذا هو الجواب عن قولهم المعدوم مراد وكل مراد ثابت
•
والجواب عن الحجة الثالثة : أن المحكوم عليه بكونه ممكنا إما أن يكون ثابتا في العدم أو لا يكون . والأول باطل لأن عندكم الذوات المعدومة يمتنع عليها التغير والخروج عن الذاتية فلا يمكن جعل الإمكان صفة لها ، وإن كان الثاني كان الإمكان وصفه لما ليس بثابت فى العدم وحينئد لا يمكنكم الاستدلال بالإمكان على كون الممكن ثابتا في العدم ـ وبالله التوفيق
Then, if you wish to tighten the discussion against the opponent, say: what do you mean by the non-existent being 'known'? If you mean by it that kind of distinction which you find in the conception of impossibilities, composites, and relatives, then that is conceded, but it does not necessitate the realization of essences in non-existence by consensus. If you mean by it something beyond that, then it is necessary to convey its conception and then establish proof for it, for we are in the position of denial in both cases.
•
As for his statement: 'the non-existent is an object of power (maqdur) and the object of power is distinct', it is weak, because the object of power is either established in non-existence or not. If it is established, then power has no effect in it at all, because making something established is impossible. If that is so, it is impossible for it to be an object of power. If it is not established, then that is an admission that the object of power is not established, and then they cannot use its being an object of power to prove that it is established. This is the response to their statement 'the non-existent is an object of will (murad) and every object of will is established.'
•
And the response to the third proof: that the thing judged to be possible (mumkin) is either established in non-existence or not. The first is false because, according to you, the non-existent essences cannot undergo change or departure from essence-ness, so possibility cannot be made an attribute for them. If the second [is the case], then possibility is an attribute of what is not established in non-existence, and then you cannot use possibility to prove that the possible is established in non-existence. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage provides three further rhetorical moves. First, the author advises tightening the argument by asking the opponent what they mean by 'known' (ma'lum). If they mean mental distinction, it is granted but does not prove extra-mental reality. If they mean something else, they must define and prove it; the author withholds assent. Second, the argument that the non-existent is an object of power (maqdur) and therefore distinct is refuted: if the object of power is already established, power has no effect; if not, then the opponent admits it is not established. The same response applies to will (irada). Third, the response to the third proof (that possibility implies establishment): if the possible is established, then possibility cannot be a new attribute because essences do not change; if not established, then possibility predicates something not established, so the argument fails. The passage ends with 'wa billahi al-tawfiq'.

Key Terms
maqdur
murad

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is an object of power or will, it must have some kind of reality or distinction.
Correction: The author shows that being an object of power or will either requires prior establishment (making power redundant) or admits non-establishment, so it does not prove extra-mental reality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the object of power is established in non-existence?
A) Power can still create it
B) Power has no effect because establishing what is already established is impossible
C) The object must be mental
D) The object is distinct by nature

Key Takeaway
The claim that the non-existent is an object of power or will does not prove its establishment; a dilemma shows the argument is unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you desire something that does not yet exist, do you treat it as if it already has reality? This lesson shows that being an object of will does not give it existence. How much of your anxiety about the future stems from confusing your desire with reality? Will you commit to trusting God's timing rather than asserting your own will?
?
Consider a time you felt powerless about something you wanted to happen. According to this dilemma, power requires an established object. Could it be that you were relying on your own power rather than God's, and that the object was not yet established? How does this redirect your reliance on God alone?
?
The opponents used the fact that God wills something to prove it is real. But the dilemma shows that this reasoning fails. Reflect on your own life: where do you falsely assume that because God wills something good for you, it must already be established in your reality? How can this realization deepen your patience and trust in divine decree?

Refuting the Argument from Possibility
Razi refutes the proof from possibility with a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of possibility (imkan) cannot prove that the possible is established in non-existence.

Core Idea
Possibility does not require extra-mental establishment.

The opponent's third argument is that the non-existent is judged to be possible (mumkin), and therefore must have some kind of reality. Imam al-Razi again uses a dilemma: if the possible is established, then possibility cannot be a new attribute because essences do not change; if it is not established, then possibility is an attribute of something not established, so the argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the third argument and its refutation.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent says that the non-existent is possible (mumkin) and therefore must be established.
3
Analyze the dilemma
The author presents two possibilities: either the possible is established or not. Each leads to a problem.
4
See why the first case fails
If established, then possibility cannot be a new attribute because essences do not change.
5
See why the second case fails
If not established, then possibility is attributed to something not established, so it does not prove establishment.

The Meaning of 'Known' for Non-Existents
Response clarifying two possible interpretations of 'ma'lum' for non-existent things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between mental distinction and extra-mental establishment in the debate over the non-existent being known, and how to apply this clarification as a rebuttal strategy.

Core Idea
'Known' for non-existents means mental distinction, not external reality.

This passage is a key methodological move in the kalam debate about the status of non-existent things. The author teaches us how to respond to an opponent who claims that non-existents are 'known' in a way that implies they have some fixed reality. By demanding clarification, we can expose whether the opponent means only a mental distinction or something more.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once without analysis. Let the author's wording sink in.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If you mean... then... If you mean... then...'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author presents two possible meanings of 'known' for non-existents. What are they? How does the author respond to each?
The first is mental distinction; the second is anything beyond that.
3
Understand the strategic implication
Why does the author concede the first but challenge the second? This is a method to block the opponent's argument.
The author says 'we are behind the prevention in both respects' – meaning he will prevent the opponent regardless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنك إن أردت تضييق الكلام على الخصم فقل ، ما الذى تعنى بكون المعدوم معلوما إن عنيت به ذلك الضرب من الامتياز الذي تجده في تصور المتنعات والمركبات والإضافيات وذلك مسلم ، لكنه لا يقتضى تقرر الماهيات في العدم بالاتفاق ، وإن عنيت به أمر وراء ذلك فلا بد من إفادة تصوره ثم إقامة الحجة عليه فإننا من وراء المنع في المقامين
Then if you wish to tighten the speech against the opponent, say: What do you mean by 'the non-existent is known'? If you mean by it that kind of distinction which you find in the conception of opposites, composites, and relatives, then that is conceded, but it does not necessitate the establishment of essences in non-existence by consensus. And if you mean by it something beyond that, then it is necessary to first convey its conception and then to present the proof for it, for we are behind the prevention in both respects.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a strategic response to the opponent who claims that the non-existent is known, implying it has some fixed reality. The author instructs the reader to ask the opponent what they mean by 'known'. If they mean the mental distinction (imtiyaz dhihni) that occurs when we conceive of opposites, composites, and relatives, then the author concedes that such mental distinction exists, but it does not necessitate that the essences have any extra-mental establishment (taqarrur) in non-existence. If the opponent means something beyond that, then they must first explain that concept and then provide proof for it. The author declares that he will block both interpretations in the two respects mentioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is known in the mind, it must have some extra-mental reality.
Correction: The author shows that 'known' can refer only to mental distinction, which does not necessitate extra-mental establishment.

Checkpoint
What does the author concede about the non-existent being known?
A) That it has extra-mental establishment.
B) That it has mental distinction only.
C) That it is impossible to know.
D) That it is known by necessity.

Key Takeaway
When an opponent says non-existents are known, we must clarify: if they mean mere mental distinction, we concede but deny any extra-mental reality; if they mean more, they must define and prove it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Four Attributes of Substances according to the Majority Mu'tazila
Razi enumerates the four attributes of substances according to the majority Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite classification of attributes of substances into four specific attributes and how they are defined.

Core Idea
Substances have exactly four attributes: substantiality, existence, spatial occupation, and position.

The Mu'tazilites who affirm attributes developed a precise ontology for substances. They distinguished between attributes of the whole (life-dependent) and attributes of individual entities. For substances, they identified exactly four attributes, each with a specific definition and causal role.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the initial division between whole and individual attributes. Then read the translation to grasp the overall structure.
2
Identify the four attributes for substances
The passage lists four attributes in order: substantiality (jawhariyya), existence (wujud), spatial occupation (tahayyuz), and position in place (husul fi al-hayyiz). Note how each is defined.
3
Understand the denial of additional attributes
The text explicitly states that an individual substance has no attribute beyond these four, so being black or white is not an attribute of the substance itself.
4
Reflect on the broader Mu'tazilite system
Consider how this classification fits into their overall theological framework, especially regarding temporal origination and the role of the agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بالصفات زعموا أن صفات الجواهر إما أن تكون عائدة إلى الجملة وهي الحياة وكل ما كان مشروطا بها أو إلى الأفراد وهي إما في الجواهر أو في الأعراض. أما الجواهر فقد أثبتوا لها صفات أربعة: أحدها الصفة الحاصلة حالتى العدم والوجود وهي الجوهرية، والثانية الوجود وهو الصفة الحاصلة بالفاعل، والثالثة التحيز وهو الصفة التابعة للحدوث والصادرة عن صفة الجوهرية بشرط الوجود، والرابعة الحصول في الحيز وهو الصفة المعللة بالمعنى. قالوا: وليس للجوهر الفرد صفة زائدة على هذه الأربع فليس له بكونه أسود وأبيض صفة وكذا القول في كل عرض غير مشروط بالحياة. وأما الأعراض فالصفات العائدة إلى الجملة غير معقولة فيها وأما العائدة إلى الآحاد فعندهم الصفة الحاصلة حالتى العدم والوجود والصفة الصادرة عنها عند الوجود صفة الوجود. فهذا هو المذهب الذي استقر جمهورهم عليه وهو قول أبي علي وأبي هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار وأبي رشيد وابن مثوبة.
Those who affirm attributes claimed that the attributes of substances are either returning to the whole (al-jumla), which is life and everything conditional upon it, or to individuals (al-afrad), and these are either in substances or in accidents. As for substances, they established for them four attributes: one of them is the attribute obtaining in the states of both non-existence and existence, which is substantiality (jawhariyya); the second is existence (wujud), which is the attribute obtained by the agent; the third is spatial occupation (tahayyuz), which is the attribute consequent upon temporal origination (huduth) and emanating from the attribute of substantiality under the condition of existence; and the fourth is position in place (husul fi al-hayyiz), which is the attribute caused by a factor (al-mu'allal bi al-ma'na). They said: The individual substance (al-jawhar al-fard) has no attribute beyond these four, so it does not have an attribute in being black or white, and similarly for every accident not conditional upon life. As for accidents, the attributes returning to the whole are not conceivable in them; as for those returning to individuals, according to them there is the attribute obtaining in the states of both non-existence and existence, and the attribute emanating from it at existence, which is the attribute of existence. This is the doctrine upon which their majority settled, and it is the statement of Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar, Abu Rashid, and Ibn Mathuba.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of attributes by the majority Mu'tazilites (those who affirm attributes). They divide attributes into two categories: those returning to the whole (e.g., life and life-dependent attributes) and those returning to individuals (substances or accidents). For substances, they list exactly four attributes: (1) substantiality (jawhariyya), which is present in both non-existence and existence; (2) existence, caused by the agent; (3) spatial occupation (tahayyuz), which originates from substantiality when existence is present; (4) position in place (husul fi al-hayyiz), caused by an extrinsic factor ('meaning'). They stress that an individual substance has no fifth attribute – e.g., being black or white is not an attribute of the substance itself but of an accident. For accidents, the whole-returning attributes are not applicable; only two individual-returning attributes: the attribute present in both non-existence and existence (i.e., the accident's essence) and the attribute of existence at the time of existence. This is the settled view of the Mu'tazilite leaders listed.

Key Terms
جوهر
صفة
جوهرية
وجود
تحيز
حصول في الحيز
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spatial occupation (tahayyuz) and position in place are the same attribute.
Correction: The passage distinguishes them: tahayyuz is the attribute consequent upon huduth and emanating from substantiality, while position is caused by a factor (ma'na). They are different.

Checkpoint
According to the majority Mu'tazila, how many attributes does an individual substance have?
A) Five, including being black or white.
B) Exactly four: substantiality, existence, spatial occupation, and position in place.
C) Three: existence, spatial occupation, and position.
D) Only one: substantiality.

Key Takeaway
The majority Mu'tazila hold that each substance has exactly four necessary attributes, no more.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very existence is, according to this lesson, an attribute given by an Agent. When was the last time you truly felt your existence as a gift, not a given? How would your daily prayers change if you began each by acknowledging that your existence is an attribute bestowed upon you, not something you own?
?
The Mu'tazila insist that a substance has exactly four attributes, no more. In your own life, what 'attributes' do you wrongly attach to your own essence—like pride in your skills or identity? Are you willing to strip away what is accidental and see yourself as you truly are in front of God, a mere substance sustained by His attributes?
?
If God's attributes are different in kind from created attributes, how careful are you when you speak of God's 'existence' or 'spatial occupation'? Do you ever fall into the trap of thinking of God as a substance with attributes like yours? What would it take to purify your theology of such unconscious anthropomorphism?

Attributes of Accidents according to the Majority Mu'tazila
Razi presents the Mu'tazilite doctrine on attributes of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite view that accidents have only two attributes: their essence and existence.

Core Idea
Accidents have no whole-returning attributes; only individual attributes: essence and existence.

After discussing substances, the text turns to accidents. The Mu'tazilites argue that the whole-returning attributes (like life) are not applicable to accidents. Instead, accidents have only two attributes: one that is present in both non-existence and existence (the accident's essence) and the attribute of existence that emanates from it.

Guided Path
1
Read the accident passage
Read the Arabic passage for accidents and its translation. Notice the structural contrast with substances.
2
Note the absence of whole-returning attributes
The text says that attributes returning to the whole are not conceivable in accidents.
3
Identify the two attributes for accidents
They are: (1) the attribute present in both non-existence and existence (the essence), and (2) the attribute of existence that emanates from it at existence.
4
Consider the authority behind this view
The passage ends by listing the scholars who held this doctrine: Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar, Abu Rashid, and Ibn Mathuba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بالصفات زعموا أن صفات الجواهر إما أن تكون عائدة إلى الجملة وهي الحياة وكل ما كان مشروطا بها أو إلى الأفراد وهي إما في الجواهر أو في الأعراض. أما الجواهر فقد أثبتوا لها صفات أربعة: أحدها الصفة الحاصلة حالتى العدم والوجود وهي الجوهرية، والثانية الوجود وهو الصفة الحاصلة بالفاعل، والثالثة التحيز وهو الصفة التابعة للحدوث والصادرة عن صفة الجوهرية بشرط الوجود، والرابعة الحصول في الحيز وهو الصفة المعللة بالمعنى. قالوا: وليس للجوهر الفرد صفة زائدة على هذه الأربع فليس له بكونه أسود وأبيض صفة وكذا القول في كل عرض غير مشروط بالحياة. وأما الأعراض فالصفات العائدة إلى الجملة غير معقولة فيها وأما العائدة إلى الآحاد فعندهم الصفة الحاصلة حالتى العدم والوجود والصفة الصادرة عنها عند الوجود صفة الوجود. فهذا هو المذهب الذي استقر جمهورهم عليه وهو قول أبي علي وأبي هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار وأبي رشيد وابن مثوبة.
Those who affirm attributes claimed that the attributes of substances are either returning to the whole (al-jumla), which is life and everything conditional upon it, or to individuals (al-afrad), and these are either in substances or in accidents. As for substances, they established for them four attributes: one of them is the attribute obtaining in the states of both non-existence and existence, which is substantiality (jawhariyya); the second is existence (wujud), which is the attribute obtained by the agent; the third is spatial occupation (tahayyuz), which is the attribute consequent upon temporal origination (huduth) and emanating from the attribute of substantiality under the condition of existence; and the fourth is position in place (husul fi al-hayyiz), which is the attribute caused by a factor (al-mu'allal bi al-ma'na). They said: The individual substance (al-jawhar al-fard) has no attribute beyond these four, so it does not have an attribute in being black or white, and similarly for every accident not conditional upon life. As for accidents, the attributes returning to the whole are not conceivable in them; as for those returning to individuals, according to them there is the attribute obtaining in the states of both non-existence and existence, and the attribute emanating from it at existence, which is the attribute of existence. This is the doctrine upon which their majority settled, and it is the statement of Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar, Abu Rashid, and Ibn Mathuba.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of attributes by the majority Mu'tazilites (those who affirm attributes). They divide attributes into two categories: those returning to the whole (e.g., life and life-dependent attributes) and those returning to individuals (substances or accidents). For substances, they list exactly four attributes: (1) substantiality (jawhariyya), which is present in both non-existence and existence; (2) existence, caused by the agent; (3) spatial occupation (tahayyuz), which originates from substantiality when existence is present; (4) position in place (husul fi al-hayyiz), caused by an extrinsic factor ('meaning'). They stress that an individual substance has no fifth attribute – e.g., being black or white is not an attribute of the substance itself but of an accident. For accidents, the whole-returning attributes are not applicable; only two individual-returning attributes: the attribute present in both non-existence and existence (i.e., the accident's essence) and the attribute of existence at the time of existence. This is the settled view of the Mu'tazilite leaders listed.

Key Terms
عرض
صفة
جملة
فرد
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many individual-returning attributes do accidents have according to the majority Mu'tazila?
A) Four, like substances.
B) Three, including life.
C) Two: the attribute present in both non-existence and existence, and the attribute of existence.
D) Only one: existence.

Key Takeaway
Accidents have only two attributes: the essence (persisting through non-existence and existence) and existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that accidents have only two attributes: essence and existence—no lasting qualities. Think of one material object you deeply value. If its attributes are only temporary, what does that say about the attachment you have invested in it? How would your heart change if you truly lived by this truth today?
?
If accidents cannot possess 'whole-returning' attributes like life or power, then only the Creator has enduring attributes. What is one created thing you are currently relying on for security or happiness? Name it specifically. Now, what concrete step can you take this week to shift that reliance to God alone, who alone possesses eternal attributes?

The Self-Evident Division: Existent and Non-Existent
Razi asserts that reason divides things into existent and non-existent with no intermediate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that reason divides everything the intellect can point to into existent and non-existent with no genuine intermediate, and that any claim of a third category is merely a verbal redefinition.

Core Idea
Reason splits reality into two: existent and non-existent — no third option.

This passage from Imam Razi establishes a foundational axiom of Islamic theology: the self-evident division of all mental concepts into existent and non-existent. It clarifies that any apparent middle ground is only a matter of definition, not a real ontological category. Understanding this division is crucial before delving into more complex theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the author's concise reasoning settle in your mind.
The Arabic uses technical terms like البديهة (self-evident) and الواسطة (intermediate).
2
Understand the self-evident judgment
Notice that Razi says reason judges (البديهة حاكمة) that everything either has realization or not. This is presented as a basic, undeniable axiom.
The division is exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
3
Reflect on the verbal nature of any supposed intermediate
Razi points out that if someone redefines 'existent' and 'non-existent', an intermediate might appear — but then the dispute becomes merely verbal (بحث لفظي), not real.
This safeguards the division from being undermined by word games.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن البديهة حاكمة بأن كل ما يشير العقل إليه فإما أن يكون له تحقق بوجه من الوجوه وإما أن لا يكون فالأول هو الموجود والثاني هو المعدوم، وعلى هذا لا واسطة بين القسمين، إلا أن يفسروا الموجود والمعدوم بغير ما ذكرنا، وحينئذ ربما حصلت الواسطة على ذلك التأويل ويصير البحث لفظيا
We have that self-evident reason judges that everything the intellect points to either has realization in some respect or does not; the first is the existent and the second is the non-existent. On this basis, there is no intermediate between the two divisions, unless they interpret 'existent' and 'non-existent' other than we have mentioned. Then perhaps the intermediate occurs on that interpretation, and the inquiry becomes verbal.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents a self-evident judgment (badīha) that everything the intellect can refer to either has some mode of realization (taḥaqquq) or does not. The first is called existent (mawjūd) and the second non-existent (maʿdūm). Consequently, there is no third category (wāsiṭa) between them. However, if one redefines the terms 'existent' and 'non-existent' differently, then an intermediate might appear, but the debate then becomes merely verbal (lafẓī).

Key Terms
البديهة
الموجود
المعدوم
الواسطة
البحث اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is a real ontological third category between existence and non-existence.
Correction: The passage denies this; any intermediate is only a verbal illusion based on redefining the terms 'existent' and 'non-existent'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does reason judge about everything the intellect points to?
A) It is either existent or non-existent with no intermediate.
B) It is either existent or non-existent or something else.
C) It is always existent.
D) It is always non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The division into existent and non-existent is exhaustive and exclusive; any apparent third category is merely a verbal redefinition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate your own existence, do you ever take for granted that you are a real being accountable to Allah? This passage reminds you that there is no middle ground — you either have realization or you do not. How does that stark division affect your sense of urgency in fulfilling your purpose?
?
The passage warns against redefining terms to create a false intermediate. In your daily life, where have you subtly redefined obedience or sin to create a comfortable middle ground between right and wrong? Can you identify one such area and commit to seeing it as it truly is?
?
If self-evident reason itself affirms this basic division, what does that imply about your innate human nature (fitrah)? When do you trust your natural reasoning about right and wrong, and when do you override it with rationalizations? Will you today accept the clear division and let it guide your actions?
