The First Argument for States (Ahwal)
Razi presents the first argument for states with three possibilities for existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the existence of states (ahwal) based on the distinction between existence and essence and the rejection of regress.

Core Idea
If existence were existent, an infinite regress would follow; thus, existence is a state.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the first argument used by proponents of states to prove that existence is not a full existent but a state. The argument relies on the shared nature of existence across differing essences and the impossibility of regress. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage from the start to 'وثبت أن الوجود لا موجود ولا معدوم'. This is the core of the first argument.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Notice that the argument presents three possibilities for existence: non-existent, existent, or neither. Understand why each is considered.
The argument eliminates the first two, leaving the third.
3
Understand the rejection of the first two
The first is impossible because existence cannot be non-existent. The second leads to infinite regress because if existence is existent, it would share existence with essences, requiring another existence.
This is the key step that forces the third option.
4
Conclude the state thesis
Since both 'existent' and 'non-existent' are impossible, existence must be a state (hal) that is neither existent nor non-existent.
This is the conclusion the argument aims for.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا بأمرين الحجة الأولى: وقد دللنا على أن الوجود وصف مشترك فيه بين الموجودات، ولا شك أن الموجودات متخالفة بماهياتها وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز فوجود الأشياء مغاير لماهياتها، ثم ذلك الوجود إما أن يكون معدوما أو موجودا أو لا معدوما ولا موجوداً والأول محال، لأن الموجودية مناقضة للمعدومية والشيء لا يكون عين نقيضه، والثانى محال إذ لو كان الوجود موجودا لكان مساويا في الوجودية للماهيات الموجودة، ولا شك في أنه مخالف لها بوجه ما، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز فالموجودية المشتركة بين الوجود وبين الماهيات الموجودة مغايرة لخصوص ماهية الوجود التى بها الامتياز، فيكون للوجود وجود آخر ويلزم التسلسل وذلك محال، وثبت أن الوجود لا موجود ولا معدوم. والجواب عن الأول أن الكلام في أن الوجود هل هو وصف مشترك فيه أم لا فقد تقدم ذكره. والآن نساعد عليه ونقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون الوجود موجودا، قوله لأنه لو كان الوجود موجودا لكان مساويا للماهيات الموجودة في الموجودية ومخالفا لها في خصوصياتها قلنا: التسلسل إنما يلزم لو اشتركا في وجه ثبوتي واختلفا في وجه آخر ثبوتي أما إذا كان الاختلاف في أمر عدمي لم يلزم التسلسل بيانه: هو أن الوجود يشارك الماهيات الموجودة في الموجودية ويخالفها بقيد عدمي، وأمر أن الوجود وحده وإن كان موجودا لكن ليس معه شيء آخر، والماهية الموجودة وإن كانت موجودة لكن لها مع مسمى الموجودية أمر آخر وهو الماهية، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لا يلزم أن يكون الوجود موجودا بوجود آخر بل يكون موجوديته عين ماهيته وعلى هذا التقدير ينقطع التسلسل.
They argued with two things. The first argument: We have demonstrated that existence is an attribute shared among existents. There is no doubt that existents differ in their essences, and that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished. So the existence of things is other than their essences. Then that existence is either non-existent, or existent, or neither existent nor non-existent. The first is impossible, because existence is contradictory to non-existence, and a thing cannot be the opposite of its opposite. The second is impossible, because if existence were existent, it would be equal to the existent essences in existence, and there is no doubt that it differs from them in some way; and that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished. So the shared existence between existence and the existent essences is other than the particular essence of existence by which it is distinguished. Thus existence would have another existence, leading to infinite regress, and that is impossible. So it is established that existence is neither existent nor non-existent. And the answer to the first: The discussion about whether existence is a shared attribute or not has already been mentioned. Now we support it and say: Why is it not permissible for existence to be existent? His statement: because if existence were existent, it would be equal to the existent essences in existence and differ from them in their particularities. We say: The regress would follow only if they share in a positive aspect and differ in another positive aspect. But if the difference is by a privative matter, the regress does not follow. Explanation: Existence shares with the existent essences in existence and differs from them by a privative condition. And the matter is that existence alone, although it is existent, has nothing else with it; while the existent essence, although it is existent, has with the name of existence another thing, which is the essence. And if the matter is thus, it does not necessitate that existence would be existent by another existence; rather, its existence is identical to its essence. On this estimation, the regress is cut off.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-ḥujja al-ūlā) from the proponents of states (aḥwāl) and its refutation. The argument begins by asserting that existence (wujūd) is an attribute (waṣf) shared among all existents. Since existents differ in their essences (mahiyyāt), and that by which they share is different from that by which they are distinguished, the existence of things must be other than their essences. Then it considers three possibilities for this existence: it is either non-existent, existent, or neither. The first is impossible because existence contradicts non-existence. The second leads to infinite regress: if existence is existent, it shares existence with existent essences but differs from them, so the shared existence would be other than the particular essence of existence, requiring another existence, and so on ad infinitum. Thus existence must be neither existent nor non-existent, which is the position of the proponents of states (that existence is a ḥāl, i.e., a state that is not a full existent).

The response (al-jawāb) begins by noting that the debate about whether existence is a shared attribute has already been addressed, then posits that existence can indeed be existent without regress. The regress only follows if the difference between existence and essences is a positive (thubūtī) attribute. However, if the difference is a privative (ʿadamī) condition, no regress occurs. Specifically, existence alone has nothing else with it, while an existent essence has both existence and its essence. Thus existence's existence is identical to its essence, so no additional existence is needed. This cuts off the regress.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية
التسلسل
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That existence must be either an existent or non-existent, with no third option.
Correction: The argument shows that a third option (a state) is necessary to avoid contradiction and regress.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument reject the possibility that existence is existent?
Because existence is non-existent
Because it leads to an infinite regress
Because existence is identical to essence
Because existents cannot share the same existence

Key Takeaway
The first argument for states uses the regress problem to prove that existence is a state, not a full existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that existence is not a full existent but a state. If your own existence is not a 'thing' that stands on its own, does that change how you view your dependence on Allah? How does that make you feel about your own permanence?
?
The argument uses the impossibility of infinite regress to prove that existence cannot be existent. Does your mind ever rely on an endless chain of reasons for your actions, or do you ultimately anchor your decisions on an absolute? What does that reveal about your need for a foundation?
?
If existence is shared among all things, and essences differ, then your essence is what makes you you. Do you often define yourself by your accidental attributes rather than your essence? How can this lesson remind you to focus on your true identity as a servant of Allah?

Refuting the Regress: Existence Can Be Existent
Razi presents the response to the regress objection using the concept of privative difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the response to the infinite regress argument works by distinguishing between positive and privative differences.

Core Idea
Regress only follows if the difference is positive; with a privative difference, no regress occurs.

The proponents of states argued that existence must be a state because otherwise a vicious regress would occur. The response shows that this regress is not inevitable. The key lies in the nature of the difference between existence and essences. Let's see how the response dissolves the regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the response carefully
Read from 'والجواب عن الأول' to the end of the passage. Understand the structure: first a preliminary remark, then the main claim, then the explanation.
2
Identify the key distinction
The response distinguishes between a positive (thubuti) difference and a privative (adami) difference. Regress only follows if the difference is positive.
This is the crucial move that stops the regress.
3
Understand the privative condition
The response explains that existence differs from essences by a privative condition: existence alone has nothing else, while essences have existence plus their essence. This difference is not a positive attribute but a lack.
4
See how regress is cut off
Because the difference is privative, the shared existence and the specific existence are not two distinct positive existences. Existence's existence is identical to its essence, so no additional existence is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا بأمرين الحجة الأولى: وقد دللنا على أن الوجود وصف مشترك فيه بين الموجودات، ولا شك أن الموجودات متخالفة بماهياتها وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز فوجود الأشياء مغاير لماهياتها، ثم ذلك الوجود إما أن يكون معدوما أو موجودا أو لا معدوما ولا موجوداً والأول محال، لأن الموجودية مناقضة للمعدومية والشيء لا يكون عين نقيضه، والثانى محال إذ لو كان الوجود موجودا لكان مساويا في الوجودية للماهيات الموجودة، ولا شك في أنه مخالف لها بوجه ما، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز فالموجودية المشتركة بين الوجود وبين الماهيات الموجودة مغايرة لخصوص ماهية الوجود التى بها الامتياز، فيكون للوجود وجود آخر ويلزم التسلسل وذلك محال، وثبت أن الوجود لا موجود ولا معدوم. والجواب عن الأول أن الكلام في أن الوجود هل هو وصف مشترك فيه أم لا فقد تقدم ذكره. والآن نساعد عليه ونقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون الوجود موجودا، قوله لأنه لو كان الوجود موجودا لكان مساويا للماهيات الموجودة في الموجودية ومخالفا لها في خصوصياتها قلنا: التسلسل إنما يلزم لو اشتركا في وجه ثبوتي واختلفا في وجه آخر ثبوتي أما إذا كان الاختلاف في أمر عدمي لم يلزم التسلسل بيانه: هو أن الوجود يشارك الماهيات الموجودة في الموجودية ويخالفها بقيد عدمي، وأمر أن الوجود وحده وإن كان موجودا لكن ليس معه شيء آخر، والماهية الموجودة وإن كانت موجودة لكن لها مع مسمى الموجودية أمر آخر وهو الماهية، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لا يلزم أن يكون الوجود موجودا بوجود آخر بل يكون موجوديته عين ماهيته وعلى هذا التقدير ينقطع التسلسل.
They argued with two things. The first argument: We have demonstrated that existence is an attribute shared among existents. There is no doubt that existents differ in their essences, and that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished. So the existence of things is other than their essences. Then that existence is either non-existent, or existent, or neither existent nor non-existent. The first is impossible, because existence is contradictory to non-existence, and a thing cannot be the opposite of its opposite. The second is impossible, because if existence were existent, it would be equal to the existent essences in existence, and there is no doubt that it differs from them in some way; and that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinguished. So the shared existence between existence and the existent essences is other than the particular essence of existence by which it is distinguished. Thus existence would have another existence, leading to infinite regress, and that is impossible. So it is established that existence is neither existent nor non-existent. And the answer to the first: The discussion about whether existence is a shared attribute or not has already been mentioned. Now we support it and say: Why is it not permissible for existence to be existent? His statement: because if existence were existent, it would be equal to the existent essences in existence and differ from them in their particularities. We say: The regress would follow only if they share in a positive aspect and differ in another positive aspect. But if the difference is by a privative matter, the regress does not follow. Explanation: Existence shares with the existent essences in existence and differs from them by a privative condition. And the matter is that existence alone, although it is existent, has nothing else with it; while the existent essence, although it is existent, has with the name of existence another thing, which is the essence. And if the matter is thus, it does not necessitate that existence would be existent by another existence; rather, its existence is identical to its essence. On this estimation, the regress is cut off.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary

The argument begins by asserting that existence (wujūd) is an attribute (waṣf) shared among all existents. Then it considers three possibilities for this existence: it is either non-existent, existent, or neither. The first is impossible because existence contradicts non-existence. The second leads to infinite regress: if existence is existent, it shares existence with existent essences but differs from them, so the shared existence would be other than the particular essence of existence, requiring another existence, and so on ad infinitum. Key Terms
القيد العدمي
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response, when does the infinite regress (tasalsul) not follow?
When the difference between the shared and the specific is a positive attribute
When the difference is a privative condition
When existence is non-existent
When existence is identical to essence

Key Takeaway
The regress is avoided because the difference between existence and essences is privative, not positive; existence's existence is identical to its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence's existence is identical to its essence, then it is self-sufficient and needs no external cause. But your own existence is not identical to your essence—you have a separate essence and existence. Does this realization humble you before the Necessary Existent, whose existence is His own essence? How does this affect your understanding of your absolute dependence on Him?
?
Razi shows that a privative difference avoids regress. Think of a time you assumed a problem would lead to infinite chain of excuses or blame. Could it be that the difference between you and God is not a positive addition but a privation in you—a lack of self-sufficiency? Will you let this shift your reliance from yourself to the One whose existence is identical to His essence?

8 min
Second Argument for States: Generic Concepts
Razi presents a two-part argument: three ways proving shared generic attributes, then a trilemma showing they must be states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the theory of states (aḥwāl), which shows that shared generic attributes must be intermediate realities between existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Shared generic concepts are real intermediate states, not mere names or non-existences.

Imam Razi presents a powerful argument for states based on our intuitive recognition of shared attributes like color-ness and knowledge-ness. The argument proceeds in two steps: first showing that essences share genuine commonalities, then proving these commonalities must be states because they cannot be fully existent nor pure non-existences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: first three ways, then a trilemma.
The argument is dense but systematic.
2
Identify the three ways of sharing
Find the three examples Razi gives: color-ness, knowledge-ness, and accident-ness. Understand why each shows a real shared attribute beyond mere naming.
Each way targets a different aspect of shared attributes.
3
Follow the trilemma
After establishing sharing, Razi offers three possibilities for the shared aspect: existent, non-existent, or neither. See why the first two are impossible, leaving the state as the only option.
This trilemma is the core logical move.
4
Connect to the theory of states
Reflect on how this argument supports the Ash'arī theory that there are intermediate realities (states) that are neither fully existent nor fully non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: الماهيات النوعية مشتركة في الأجناس وذلك يوجب القول بالحال. بيان الأول من وجوه أحدها: أن السواد والبياض اشتركا في اللونية، وليس الاشتراك في مجرد الاسم لانا لو سمينا السواد والحركة باسم واحد ولم نضع للسواد والبياض اسما واحدا لكنا نعلم بالضرورة أن بين السواد والبياض من المجانسة ما ليس بين السواد والحركة، ولذلك فإن الاشتراك اللفظى لا يكون مفردا في اللغات بأسرها، وهذا النوع من الاشتراك معلوم لكل العقلاء. وثانيها: أن العلوم المتعلقة بالمعلومات المغايرة مختلفة، ثم إنا نحدد العلم بحد واحد يندرج فيه العلم بالقديم والعلم بالمحدث والعلم بالجوهر والعلم بالعرض، والمحدود ليس هو اللفظ بل المعنى، فعلمنا أن العالمية وصف مشترك فيه بين هذه الماهيات المختلفة. وثالثها: أنا نقول الممكن إما جوهر وإما عرض، فلولا أن العرضية وصف واحد وإلا لم يكن التقسيم صحيحا، كما أن قولنا الممكن إما جوهر وإما سواد وإما بياض ليس تقسيما منحصرا. بيان الثاني: أنه إذا ثبت أن هذه الماهيات مشتركة من بعض الوجوه ومختلفة من وجوه أخر فالوجهان إما أن يكونا موجودين أو معدومين أو لا موجودين ولا معدومين، فالأول محال وإلا لزم قيام العرض بالعرض، والثاني باطل لأنا نعلم بالضرورة أن هذه الأمور ليست إعداما صرفا، فيقي الثالث وهو المطلوب. ثم قالت النفاة: رأينا حامل أدلة مثبتى الأحوال على اختلافها راجعا إلى حرف واحد، وهو أن الحقائق مختلفة بخصوصياتها ومشتركة في عمومياتها وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الاختلاف، ثم يبنوا أن ذلك ليس بوجود ولا معدوم فأثبتوا الواسطة قالوا: وهذا يقتضى أن يكون للحال حال آخر إلى غير النهاية، لأن هذه الأحوال التي بينوها لا شك أنها متخالفة في خصوصياتها ومتساوية في عموم كونها حالا وما به المشاركة غير ما به التمايز، فيلزم أن يكون للحال حال إلى غير النهاية. أجاب المبتون من وجهين: الأول وهو الذي عليه تعويل الجمهور أن الحال لا يوصف بالتماثل والاختلاف والثاني التزام التسلسل. فقالت النفاة: أما الأول فضعيف جدا لأن كل أمرين يشير العقل إليهما، فإما أن يكون المتصور من أحدهما هو المتصور من الآخر، أو لا يكون والأول هو المثل والثاني هو المخالف، فعلمنا أن القول بإثبات أمرين لا يوصفان بالتماثل والاختلاف جهالة. أما الثاني وهو التزام التسلسل فباطل، لأنا متى جوزناه انسد علينا إبطال حوادث لا أول لها وانسد باب إثبات الصانع القديم وكل ذلك جهالة هذا محصل كلام الفريقين.
The second argument: Specific essences share in genera, and that necessitates the assertion of the state (al-ḥāl). Elucidation of the first [point] from aspects: One of them: Blackness and whiteness share in color-ness (al-lawniyya), and the sharing is not merely in name, because if we called blackness and motion by one name and did not place for blackness and whiteness one name, we would know necessarily that between blackness and whiteness there is a homogeneity (mujānasa) that is not between blackness and motion. Therefore, verbal sharing is not found in all languages, and this type of sharing is known to all rational people. Second: The sciences related to different known objects are different; then we define knowledge by one definition that includes knowledge of the eternal, knowledge of the originated, knowledge of substance, and knowledge of accident. The defined is not the word but the meaning, so we know that knowledge-ness (al-ʿālamiyya) is a shared attribute among these different essences. Third: We say the possible is either substance or accident. Were accident-ness (al-ʿaraḍiyya) not one attribute, the division would not be valid, just as our saying 'the possible is either substance or blackness or whiteness' is not an exclusive division. Elucidation of the second [point]: When it is established that these essences share in some aspects and differ in others, the two aspects are either existent or non-existent or neither existent nor non-existent. The first is impossible, otherwise it would entail the accident's subsisting in an accident; the second is false because we know necessarily that these matters are not pure non-existences; so the third remains, which is what is sought. Then the deniers said: We see that the burden of the proofs of the proponents of states, despite their variation, returns to one point: that realities differ in their particularities and share in their universalities, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ. Then they build that that [shared aspect] is neither existent nor non-existent, so they affirm the intermediate. They said: And this necessitates that a state would have another state ad infinitum, because these states that they have elucidated are undoubtedly different in their particularities and equal in the generality of being a state, and that by which they participate is other than that by which they are distinguished, so it would be necessary that the state has a state ad infinitum. The proponents answered in two ways: The first, which is the reliance of the majority, is that the state is not described by similarity and difference; the second is the acceptance of the regress. Then the deniers said: As for the first, it is very weak because every two things that the intellect points to — either the conception of one is the conception of the other or not; the first is the similar and the second is the different. So we know that asserting two things that are not described by similarity and difference is ignorance. As for the second, the acceptance of the regress, it is false because whenever we allow it, the refutation of beginningless events is closed to us, and the door of proving the eternal Maker is closed — all that is ignorance. This is the summary of the two groups' discourse.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage contains the second argument (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) for the theory of states (aḥwāl), the critique from the deniers (al-nufāt), and the proponents' two responses.

The second argument has two parts: (1) a proof that specific essences share in generic concepts, and (2) a proof that these shared aspects are states (aḥwāl). The first part is shown through three ways: (i) blackness and whiteness share in 'color-ness' (lawniyya) — not just in name, but in a real homogeneity; (ii) different types of knowledge share a common definition of 'knowledge-ness' (ʿālamiyya); (iii) the division of the possible into substance and accident works only because 'accident-ness' (ʿaraḍiyya) is a single attribute. The second part argues that the shared aspect and the differentiating aspect must each be either existent, non-existent, or neither. The first two options are impossible, so they must be neither existent nor non-existent — i.e., a state (ḥāl).

Then the deniers (al-nufāt) object that this method commits its proponents to an infinite regress: if the shared aspect is a state, and states differ from each other in their particularities while sharing in being a state, then the shared aspect of being a state would itself be a state, and so on infinitely.

The proponents (muthbitūn) respond in two ways. The first, preferred by the majority, denies that states are describable as similar (tamāthul) or different (ikhtilāf) at all, thus blocking the regress. The second accepts the regress as permissible (iltizām al-tasalsul).

The deniers then refute both responses. The first is weak because reason inevitably judges that any two things are either similar or different, so denying that is ignorance. The second is disastrous because accepting infinite regress would undermine the impossibility of beginningless events and the proof of the eternal Creator.

Key Terms

الأجناس
الحال
اللونية
العالمية
العرضية
التماثل والاختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think shared attributes like 'color' are just mental concepts or names with no extra-mental reality.
Correction: Razi argues that the sharing is real and known necessarily to all rational people, not merely verbal. The trilemma then shows that this real sharing cannot be a full existent or a pure non-existence, hence it is a state.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three ways Razi uses to prove shared generic attributes?
A) Blackness and whiteness share in color-ness.
B) Different types of knowledge share a single definition.
C) The division of the possible into substance and accident requires accident-ness to be a single attribute.
D) The concept of a state applies to all attributes equally.

Key Takeaway
The second argument for states proves that shared generic attributes are real intermediate realities (states) because they cannot be fully existent nor pure non-existences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you accept that shared attributes like 'knowledge-ness' are real states that exist between existence and non-existence, how does that affect your understanding of your own knowledge compared to Allah's knowledge? Does it make you more humble, and if so, what concrete action can you take today to acknowledge that gap?
?
The argument shows that what we share in common with others (e.g., humanity, rationality) is not just a name but a real state. Think of a person you have difficulty relating to. How would treating your shared humanity as a real, intermediate state change your attitude toward them? What one step can you take this week to act on that shared reality?
?
Razi argues that shared aspects cannot be pure non-existences. In your own life, are there shared values or principles you treat as non-existent conveniences rather than real commitments? Name one such value and a specific situation where you will act on it as a real state.

8 min
Deniers' Critique and Proponents' Responses
Razi presents the deniers' infinite regress objection and the proponents' two responses with rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deniers' charge of infinite regress against the theory of states and the proponents' two responses, along with the deniers' rebuttals.

Core Idea
The charge of infinite regress is met by denying that states are subject to similarity and difference.

After presenting the second argument, Razi reports the deniers' (al-nufāt) critique that the theory of states leads to an infinite regress, because states themselves would require a further shared state. The proponents respond in two ways: one denying that states are similar or different, the other accepting the regress. The deniers then rebut both responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Read the deniers' objection carefully. Notice how they reduce all proofs of states to one point: the shared aspect is neither existent nor non-existent, leading to a regress.
The deniers are attacking the foundational logic of the state theory.
2
Understand the regress argument
The deniers argue: if a state is the shared aspect of particularities, and states themselves share in being states, then there must be a further state of state-ness, and so on infinitely.
This is a classic regress objection.
3
Examine the first response
The proponents' majority response: states are not described by similarity (tamāthul) or difference (ikhtilāf), so the regress is blocked at the first level.
This denies a premise of the regress.
4
Examine the second response and refutations
The second response accepts the regress (iltizām al-tasalsul). The deniers rebut both: the first is 'weak' because any two things are either similar or different; the second is 'ignorance' because it destroys proofs of the Creator.
The deniers' rebuttals are meant to show the inadequacy of both responses.

The second argument: Specific essences share in genera, and that necessitates the assertion of the state (al-ḥāl). Elucidation of the first [point] from aspects: One of them: Blackness and whiteness share in color-ness (al-lawniyya), and the sharing is not merely in name, because if we called blackness and motion by one name and did not place for blackness and whiteness one name, we would know necessarily that between blackness and whiteness there is a homogeneity (mujānasa) that is not between blackness and motion. Therefore, verbal sharing is not found in all languages, and this type of sharing is known to all rational people. The defined is not the word but the meaning, so we know that knowledge-ness (al-ʿālamiyya) is a shared attribute among these different essences. Third: We say the possible is either substance or accident. Were accident-ness (al-ʿaraḍiyya) not one attribute, the division would not be valid, just as our saying 'the possible is either substance or blackness or whiteness' is not an exclusive division. Elucidation of the second [point]: When it is established that these essences share in some aspects and differ in others, the two aspects are either existent or non-existent or neither existent nor non-existent. The first is impossible, otherwise it would entail the accident's subsisting in an accident; the second is false because we know necessarily that these matters are not pure non-existences; so the third remains, which is what is sought. Then they build that that [shared aspect] is neither existent nor non-existent, so they affirm the intermediate. They said: And this necessitates that a state would have another state ad infinitum, because these states that they have elucidated are undoubtedly different in their particularities and equal in the generality of being a state, and that by which they participate is other than that by which they are distinguished, so it would be necessary that the state has a state ad infinitum. So we know that asserting two things that are not described by similarity and difference is ignorance. Commentary
This passage contains the second argument (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) for the theory of states (aḥwāl), the critique from the deniers (al-nufāt), and the proponents' two responses.

The second argument has two parts: (1) a proof that specific essences share in generic concepts, and (2) a proof that these shared aspects are states (aḥwāl). The first part is shown through three ways: (i) blackness and whiteness share in 'color-ness' (lawniyya) — not just in name, but in a real homogeneity; (ii) different types of knowledge share a common definition of 'knowledge-ness' (ʿālamiyya); (iii) the division of the possible into substance and accident works only because 'accident-ness' (ʿaraḍiyya) is a single attribute. The second part argues that the shared aspect and the differentiating aspect must each be either existent, non-existent, or neither. The first two options are impossible, so they must be neither existent nor non-existent — i.e., a state (ḥāl).

Then the deniers (al-nufāt) object that this method commits its proponents to an infinite regress: if the shared aspect is a state, and states differ from each other in their particularities while sharing in being a state, then the shared aspect of being a state would itself be a state, and so on infinitely.

The proponents (muthbitūn) respond in two ways. The first, preferred by the majority, denies that states are describable as similar (tamāthul) or different (ikhtilāf) at all, thus blocking the regress. The second accepts the regress as permissible (iltizām al-tasalsul).

The deniers then refute both responses. The first is weak because reason inevitably judges that any two things are either similar or different, so denying that is ignorance. The second is disastrous because accepting infinite regress would undermine the impossibility of beginningless events and the proof of the eternal Creator.

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
What is the main rebuttal of the deniers against the proponents' first response (that states are not described by similarity and difference)?
A) It leads to an infinite regress.
B) It contradicts the definition of a state.
C) Reason judges that any two things are either similar or different, so denying that is ignorance.
D) It cannot be proven from scripture.

Key Takeaway
The proponents of states defend against the regress by asserting that states are not subject to similarity and difference, thus stopping the regress at the first level.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The deniers warn that accepting infinite regress would 'close the door of proving the eternal Maker'. Are there intellectual positions or habits in your own life that, if consistently followed, would lead you to deny something you hold as foundational? Are you willing to examine them honestly?
?
The proponents' main response denies that states are similar or different—a counterintuitive move that challenges a basic logical assumption. When your beliefs are attacked from a seemingly airtight argument, are you willing to question the categories and assumptions of that argument, even if it feels like 'ignorance' to others?
?
The deniers call the proponents' second response (accepting regress) 'ignorance' because it undermines proofs of God's existence. Have you ever held a theological view that, when pushed to its logical conclusion, would threaten a core conviction? What did you do—compromise, retreat, or dig deeper?

Second and Third Proofs for Shared Attributes Across Essences
Al-Razi presents two proofs that different essences share a common attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the single definition of knowledge and the exhaustive division of the possible to demonstrate that different essences share common attributes.

Core Idea
Two rational proofs show that essences share attributes through definition and division.

Al-Razi continues his argument for shared attributes by offering two further proofs. The first examines how a single definition of knowledge applies to diverse objects of knowledge, implying a shared attribute of 'knowingness.' The second uses the logical division of the possible into substance and accident, showing that the unity of the division requires a shared attribute 'accidentness.' Both proofs reinforce the idea that common attributes are rationally necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the two proofs carefully.
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: two separate arguments introduced by 'Second:' and 'Third:'.
Pay attention to the terms 'العالمية' and 'العرضية' as key shared attributes.
2
Analyze the second proof: definition of knowledge.
Al-Razi argues that despite different objects of knowledge (eternal, contingent, substance, accident), knowledge is defined by a single definition. Since definition applies to meaning, not word, 'knowingness' is a shared attribute. Understand why this implies commonality.
The proof hinges on the unity of definition across diverse objects.
3
Analyze the third proof: division of the possible.
The exhaustive division of the possible into substance and accident works only if 'accidentness' is a single attribute covering all accidents. Otherwise, the division would be incomplete, like dividing into substance, blackness, whiteness. Recognize the logical necessity of a shared attribute for exhaustive division.
This proof uses the logical principle that a proper division requires a single criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : أن العلوم المتعلقة بالمعلومات المغايرة مختلفة ، ثم إنا نحدد العلم بحد واحد يندرج فيه العلم بالقديم والعلم بالمحدث والعلم بالجوهر والعلم بالمرض [؟]، والمحدود ليس هو اللفظ بل المعنى ، فعلمنا أن العالمية وصف مشترك فيه بين هذه الماهيات المختلفة. وثالثها : أنا تقول الممكن إما جوهر وإما عرض، فلولا أن العرضية وصف واحد وإلا لم يكن التقسيم را [؟]، كما أن قولنا الممكن إما جوهر وإما سواد وإما بياض ليس تقسيما منحصرا .
Second: The sciences related to different known things are different. Then we define knowledge by a single definition that includes knowledge of the eternal, knowledge of the contingent, knowledge of the substance, and knowledge of the accident [؟]. The defined is not the word but the meaning. So we know that 'knowingness' is a shared description among these different essences. Third: We say: the possible is either substance or accident. If not for the fact that 'accidentness' is a single description, the division would not be [؟], as our saying 'the possible is either substance or blackness or whiteness' is not an exhaustive division.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs (the second and third overall) for the existence of a shared attribute (al-wasf al-mushtarak) across different essences. In the second proof, al-Razi argues that despite the diversity of the objects of knowledge (e.g., the eternal, the contingent, substance, accident), we can still define 'knowledge' (al-ilm) by a single definition that applies to all instances. Since the defined is the meaning (al-ma'na) and not the word, this implies that 'knowingness' (al-alamiyya) is a single attribute shared by these different essences. In the third proof, he points out that the division of the possible (al-mumkin) into substance (jawhar) and accident (arad) is only valid because 'accidentness' (al-ardiyya) is a single attribute covering all accidents. If it were not a unified description, the division would not be exhaustive, as illustrated by the hypothetical division into substance, blackness, and whiteness, which fails to cover all possibilities (e.g., other accidents). Thus both proofs establish that different essences share common attributes.

Key Terms
العلوم
المعلومات
الحد
العالمية
الممكن
جوهر
عرض
العرضية
التقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proofs are linguistic rather than ontological, focusing on words rather than realities.
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly states that the defined is the meaning, not the word, so the proofs concern real attributes shared by essences.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the third proof about dividing the possible into substance and accident?
A) To show that 'accidentness' is a single attribute shared by all accidents.
B) To show that the possible must be a substance.
C) To show that the division into substance and accident is arbitrary.
D) To show that blackness and whiteness are not accidents.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi presents two logical proofs—from the single definition of knowledge and the exhaustive division of the possible—to establish that different essences share common attributes.

Classification of Shared Attributes and the Accident-in-Accident Problem
Commentator classifies shared attributes and addresses accident-in-accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator classifies shared attributes into positive and negative, and further subdivides positive attributes into those included in the concept and those not, and why each category avoids the problem of accident inhering in accident.

Core Idea
Shared attributes are classified to avoid the fallacy of accident-in-accident.

The commentator begins by analyzing the nature of shared attributes, which are attributes common to multiple entities. He divides them into positive and negative categories, and further distinguishes between positive attributes that are part of the concept and those that are merely accidental. This classification is essential for understanding why shared attributes do not necessarily lead to the logical problem of an accident inhering in another accident. In this lesson, we will explore these categories and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
Read the passage and note that shared attributes are first divided into positive (thubutiyya) and negative (salbiyya). Look for the phrase 'لا تخلو من أن تكون ثبوتية أو لا تسكون' and note the distinction.
2
Distinguish included vs non-included positive attributes
Find where the commentator further divides positive attributes into those included in the concept (dakhila) and those not included (ghayr dakhila). For each type, understand the example and why it does not cause accident-in-accident.
3
Understand why negative attributes are safe
Read the part about negative attributes and how they are 'not fixed' (ghayr thabita), so they do not entail accident-in-accident.
This completes the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : الصفات المشتركة لا تخلو من أن تكون ثبوتية أو لا تسكون ، والثبوتية لا تخلو إما أن تكون داخلة في مفهومات ما يشترك فى تلك الصفات أولا تكون ، والداخلة تكون كاللون الذى يشترك فيه السواد والبياض وتكون هى جزء من مفهوم السوادية والبياضية ، والجزء لا يكون عرضا قائما بالمركب فلا يلزم من اتصاف المختلفات برما قيام العرض بالعرض ، وغير الداخلة كالعرض الذى يوصف به السواد والحركة ، والعرض هو عارض لهما غير داخل في مفهومهما ، وعروض الشيء للشيء لا يكون قيام عرض بعرض ولا يلزم من كون صفة مشتركة عارضة لمختلفين قيامهما بهما إلا بدليل منفصل ، وأما الصفات السلبية فهي غير ثابتة ولا يلزم الاتصاف بهما قيام عرض بعرض ، وأما تزييف قول المثبتين أن الحال لا يوصف بالتماثل والاختلاف فليس بوارد عليهم لأنهم يقولون المثلان ذاتان يفهم منهما معنى واحد والمختلفان ذاتان لا يفهم منهما معنى واحد، والحال ليس بذات ولا ذات ذات فلا يوصف بالتماثل والاختلاف .
I say: the shared attributes are not free from being either positive (thubutiyya) or not [positive]. The positive attributes are not free from being either included in the concepts of what shares in those attributes or not. The included [type] is like color, in which blackness and whiteness share, and it is part of the concept of blackness and whiteness. The part is not an accident subsisting in the composite, so the description of the different things with it does not entail accident inhering in accident. The non-included [type] is like the accident by which blackness and motion are described; the accident is an accidental to both, not included in their concepts, and the accidentalness of a thing to another is not accident inhering in accident. Nor does it entail, from a shared attribute being accidental to two different things, that they [i.e., the two different things] subsist in it except by a separate proof. As for negative attributes (as-sifat as-salbiyya), they are not fixed, and being described by them does not entail accident inhering in accident. As for the refutation of the affirmers' statement that the state is not described by similarity and difference, it is not valid against them because they say: two similars (mithlan) are two essences (dhatan) from which one meaning is understood, and two different things are two essences from which one meaning is not understood. The state is neither an essence nor the essence of an essence, so it is not described by similarity and difference.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the nature of shared attributes and their relation to states. The commentator divides shared attributes into two categories: positive and non-positive (negative). Positive attributes are further divided into those that are included in the concepts of the things sharing them (like color shared by black and white) and those that are not included (like an accident that is accidental to both, such as motion being attributed to both blackness and movement). For the included type, the shared attribute is a part of the concept, and a part is not an accident subsisting in the composite whole, so no accident-in-accident problem arises. For the non-included type, the shared attribute is accidental to both, but that accidentalness is not an accident inhering in another accident unless proven separately. Negative attributes are not fixed (ghayr thabita), so they do not cause that problem. Finally, the commentator addresses the criticism that states cannot be described as similar or different: the affirmers (proponents of states) define similars as two essences from which one meaning is understood, and different things as essences from which different meanings are understood. Since a state is not an essence (dhat) nor the essence of an essence, it falls outside that classification, so the criticism is invalid.

Key Terms
صفات مشتركة
ثبوتية
سلبية
داخلة
غير داخلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All shared attributes lead to the problem of accident inhering in accident.
Correction: The commentator explains that positive attributes included in the concept do not cause it, non-included require a separate proof, and negative attributes do not cause it at all.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which type of shared attribute does NOT entail accident inhering in accident?
A) Positive attributes that are not included in the concept.
B) Positive attributes that are included in the concept.
C) Negative attributes.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Shared attributes are classified into positive (with two subtypes) and negative, and none of them necessarily entail the fallacy of accident-in-accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the attributes of Allah, do you sometimes slip into thinking that because He shares a name with a created quality, He must be like that creation? How can this classification guard your heart against tashbih?
?
There is a temptation to take intellectual shortcuts—assuming that if two things share a property, they must be the same in essence. Where in your own thinking about faith have you fallen for this? What step of this classification can help you repent of that haste?
?
Negative attributes (salbiyya) are 'not fixed', yet we still describe Allah by them. Does your heart truly embrace that Allah's 'not being unjust' is a real perfection, or do you wish He had a positive attribute of 'justice' like ours? How does this lesson reshape your understanding of divine perfection?

Refuting the Criticism that States Cannot Be Similar or Different
Commentator refutes criticism that states cannot be similar/different.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the affirmers of states against the criticism that states cannot be described as similar or different.

Core Idea
Similarity and difference apply to essences, not to states.

The commentator addresses a criticism leveled by opponents of the doctrine of states (ahwal). The critics argue that if states are real but not essences, then they cannot be described as similar or different, which would undermine arguments based on similarity and difference. The commentator shows that this criticism fails because the definitions of similarity and difference only apply to essences (dhat), and states are not essences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criticism
Read the first part where the commentator mentions 'tazyif' (refutation) of the affirmers' statement. The criticism is that the state (hal) cannot be described by similarity and difference.
2
Understand the affirmers' definitions
The commentator quotes the affirmers: two similars are two essences from which one meaning is understood; two different things are essences from which different meanings are understood. This definition is key.
3
See why the criticism fails
Since the state is not an essence nor the essence of an essence, it falls outside the definition, so the criticism is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The Philosophers' View on Genera and Differentiae
Razi presents philosophers' view and objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that genera and differentiae exist only in minds and the objection raised against them.

Core Idea
Genera and differentiae are mental concepts, not external realities.

The philosophers offered a unique perspective on universals, arguing that genera and differentiae are not found in the external world but are merely mental constructs. This passage from Imam Razi presents their view along with an initial objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' statement
Read the Arabic sentence where the philosophers state their view. Notice they say genera and differentiae exist in minds, not in concrete entities.
This is their core claim.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that if mental judgments must correspond to external reality, then proponents of states (who affirm real shared attributes) are correct; if not, the philosophers' view is ignorance.
The objection challenges the validity of their view.
3
Reflect on the argument
Ask yourself: does the philosophers' view lead to ignorance? The commentator will answer in the next lesson.
This sets up the commentator's response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفلاسفة في هذا الباب طريق آخر وهو أنهم قالوا الأجناس والفصول التي بها تتقوم والأنواع البسيطة في الخارج موجودات في الأذهان لا في الأعيان فقيل لهم الحكم الذهنى إن كان مطابقاً للخارج عاد كلام مثبتى الحال وإلا فهو جهل ولا عبرة به - وبالله التوفيق (۱) أقول : الأجناس أو الفصول ليست بتصديقات إنما هي تصورت امفردة ولا يجب فيما لا يشتمل على الحسكم بمطابقة الخارج أن يكون مطابقاً وإلا فكان جهلا، وذلك أن الجهل المركب حكم على الواقع بخلاف الواقع، وفي التصور المفرد لا يعتبر المطابقة ولا خلافها بل يعتبر فيما له أجناس وفصول أن يكون فيماله حيثيات يمكن للمقول تعقل الأجناس والفصول منهما، ولذلك يسلبان من الواجب الوجود لامتناع أن يكون فيه حيثيتان، وليس معنى الاشتراك إلا أن يكون شيء واحد فى الخارج موجوداً في شيئين معا، أو يصف منه في كل واحد منهما أو خارجاً عنهما وهما متصفان به
And the philosophers in this matter have another way: they said that genera (ajnas) and differentiae (fusul) by which simple species (anwa') are constituted exist in the external world as existents in minds (adhhan), not in concrete entities (a'yan). So it was said to them: if the mental judgment (hukm dhihni) is in accordance (mutabiq) with the external, then the statement of the proponents of the state returns; otherwise it is ignorance (jahl) and no attention is paid to it. And with God is success. (1) I say: genera or differentiae are not affirmations (tasdiqat); rather they are single conceptions (tasawwurat mufrada). And that which does not include judgment of correspondence with the external does not have to be corresponding; otherwise it would be ignorance. That is because compound ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) is a judgment upon reality contrary to reality, but in the single conception (tasawwur mufrad), correspondence or its opposite is not considered. Rather, consideration is given to that which has genera and species: that it has aspects (haythiyyat) by which the intellect can conceive the genera and differentiae from them. Therefore, they are negated (yuslaban) from the Necessary Existence, because it is impossible for there to be two aspects in Him. And the meaning of sharing (ishtirak) is only that one thing in the external exists in two things together, or is described from each of them, or is external to them while they are described by it.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' alternative approach to universals and then provides a commentary. The philosophers hold that genera and differentiae, which constitute species, exist only in minds, not in external reality. The objection raised against them is that if mental judgments must correspond to external reality, then the proponents of states (who affirm real shared attributes) are correct; if not, then the philosophers' view is ignorance and invalid. The commentator (the 'I say') responds by distinguishing between conceptions (tasawwurat) and affirmations (tasdiqat). Genera and differentiae are single conceptions, not judgments, so they do not involve a claim of correspondence that could be true or false. They are merely conceptual tools for understanding. For things that have genera and species, the intellect can conceive aspects (haythiyyat) from which the genera and differentiae are abstracted. This does not apply to God, who has no composition. The commentator also defines 'sharing' (ishtirak) as one thing existing in two external things, or being predicated of each, or being external to both while they are described by it.

Key Terms
فلاسفة
أجناس
فصول
أنواع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That genera and differentiae are real external entities existing independently of the mind.
Correction: The philosophers assert they exist only mentally, not externally.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, where do genera and differentiae exist?
A) In concrete external entities
B) Only in minds
C) Both in minds and external
D) Neither in minds nor external

Key Takeaway
The philosophers claim that genera and differentiae exist solely in the mind, not in external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of categories like 'human' or 'animal', do you instinctively assume they exist as real entities outside your mind? What would change in your daily life if you saw them only as useful mental constructs?
?
The objection warns that if mental judgments do not correspond to external reality, they are ignorance. Think of a belief you once held about yourself or the world that you later discovered was purely a mental construct with no external basis. How did you handle that realization, and what did you learn?
?
The philosophers' view separates mental existence from concrete existence. Where in your own thinking do you tend to conflate the two—assuming a category or label you use in your mind is objectively real? Write down one concrete step you can take today to test whether that mental category corresponds to something external.

The Distinction Between Conception and Affirmation
Commentator distinguishes conception from affirmation and applies to divine transcendence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the philosophers by distinguishing conception from affirmation.

Core Idea
Genera and differentiae are single conceptions, not judgments, so they don't require external correspondence.

The commentator responds to the objection by clarifying that genera and differentiae are not judgments (tasdiqat) but single conceptions (tasawwurat). This distinction saves the philosophers' view from the charge of ignorance and explains why God cannot have genera.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's response
Read the Arabic from 'I say' onwards. Notice the key distinction between tasawwurat and tasdiqat.
This distinction is central to the defense.
2
Understand why conception avoids ignorance
The commentator explains that single conceptions do not involve a claim about reality that could be false, unlike compound ignorance which is a false judgment.
Conceptions are neutral; only judgments can be true or false.
3
Apply to God's nature
Since God has no composition or aspects, genera and differentiae cannot be attributed to Him. This upholds divine transcendence.
The commentator uses this to show the limits of such concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفلاسفة في هذا الباب طريق آخر وهو أنهم قالوا الأجناس والفصول التي بها تتقوم والأنواع البسيطة في الخارج موجودات في الأذهان لا في الأعيان فقيل لهم الحكم الذهنى إن كان مطابقاً للخارج عاد كلام مثبتى الحال وإلا فهو جهل ولا عبرة به - وبالله التوفيق (۱) أقول : الأجناس أو الفصول ليست بتصديقات إنما هي تصورت امفردة ولا يجب فيما لا يشتمل على الحسكم بمطابقة الخارج أن يكون مطابقاً وإلا فكان جهلا، وذلك أن الجهل المركب حكم على الواقع بخلاف الواقع، وفي التصور المفرد لا يعتبر المطابقة ولا خلافها بل يعتبر فيما له أجناس وفصول أن يكون فيماله حيثيات يمكن للمقول تعقل الأجناس والفصول منهما، ولذلك يسلبان من الواجب الوجود لامتناع أن يكون فيه حيثيتان، وليس معنى الاشتراك إلا أن يكون شيء واحد فى الخارج موجوداً في شيئين معا، أو يصف منه في كل واحد منهما أو خارجاً عنهما وهما متصفان به

The objection raised against them is that if mental judgments must correspond to external reality, then the proponents of states (who affirm real shared attributes) are correct; if not, then the philosophers' view is ignorance and invalid. The commentator (the 'I say') responds by distinguishing between conceptions (tasawwurat) and affirmations (tasdiqat). Genera and differentiae are single conceptions, not judgments, so they do not involve a claim of correspondence that could be true or false. They are merely conceptual tools for understanding. For things that have genera and species, the intellect can conceive aspects (haythiyyat) from which the genera and differentiae are abstracted. This does not apply to God, who has no composition. The commentator also defines 'sharing' (ishtirak) as one thing existing in two external things, or being predicated of each, or being external to both while they are described by it.

Key Terms
تصديقات
تصورات مفردة
جهل مركب
حيثيات
اشتراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That single conceptions can be judged as true or false.
Correction: Single conceptions do not involve a truth claim; only affirmations do.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the philosophers' view does not lead to ignorance?
A) Because genera and differentiae are judgments that correspond to reality.
B) Because genera and differentiae are single conceptions, not judgments requiring correspondence.
C) Because philosophers deny the existence of genera altogether.
D) Because the Necessary Existence has many aspects.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between conception and affirmation shows that mental concepts do not require external correspondence, preserving the philosophers' view and upholding divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator is adamant that no genera or species can be conceived of Allah because He has no aspects. When you describe Allah with attributes like 'Merciful' or 'Knower', do you ever imagine these as parts of His essence? In what specific prayer or thought today will you remind yourself that His attributes are not aspects but are identical with His essence?
?
Compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) is a judgment that contradicts reality but is held with certainty. Can you identify one belief you have held—about your own nature, about God, or about someone else—that was actually a compound ignorance? What current assumption might you be holding with certainty that you have not tested against the truth?
?
The passage teaches that single conceptions, unlike judgments, do not require external correspondence. How often do you mentally categorize people or situations using concepts (e.g., 'successful person', 'bad day') and then treat those concepts as if they are objectively true judgments? What is one concept you have been applying to yourself or others that you need to recognize as merely a conception, not a reality?

Caused and Non-Caused States, and the Nature of Existence
Razi classifies states and notes sectarian positions on existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of states into caused (mu'allal) and non-caused (ghayr mu'allal), and the differing views on existence (wujud) between the Ash'ari proponents of states and the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
States are either grounded in an attribute or not; existence itself is disputed.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a key classification from the proponents of states (ahwal). He distinguishes states that are caused by an existing attribute from those that are not. He then notes an agreement on essences and a disagreement on existence itself, linking to the debate about the non-existent being a thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall structure and key terms.
Notice the paired terms: 'mu'allal' vs 'ghayr mu'allal', and the examples given.
2
Identify the two types of states
Find the definitions and examples for caused state (al-hal al-mu'allal) and non-caused state (al-hal ghayr al-mu'allal). Understand why 'blackness of black' is an example of the latter.
The caused state depends on an existent attribute; the non-caused does not.
3
Understand the positions on existence
Read the part about agreement on essences and then the two views on existence. Note how this relates to the claim that the non-existent is a thing.
This connects the classification to a broader theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا ثبوت الحالية للشيء إما أن يكون معللا بموجود قائم بذلك الشيء كالعالمية المعللة بالعلم أولا يكون كذلك كسوادية السواد ، والأول هو الحال المعلل والثانى الحال الغير المعلل ، واتفقوا على أن الموجودات متساوية في الذوات ومختلفة في هذه الأحوال ، وأما الوجود فزهم مثبتو الحال منا أنه نفس الذات وزعمت المعتزلة أنه صفة ، والقول بإثبات كون المعدوم شيئاً بناء على هذا
They said: the affirmation of being a state (al-haliyya) for a thing is either caused (mu'allal) by an existent subsisting in that thing, like 'being knowing' (al-'alimiyya) caused by knowledge, or not like that, like the blackness of black (sawadiyyat as-sawad). The first is the caused state (al-hal al-mu'allal) and the second is the non-caused state (al-hal ghayr al-mu'allal). And they agreed that existents are equal in essences (dhawat) and differ in these states. As for existence (al-wujud), the proponents of states among us claim it is the essence itself (nafs adh-dhat), while the Mu'tazila claimed it is an attribute (sifa). And the statement affirming that the non-existent is a thing is based on this.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of states (ahwal) according to the proponents. They divide states into two kinds: (1) 'caused state' (al-hal al-mu'allal) – a state that is grounded in an existent attribute inhering in the thing, such as the state of being knowing (al-'alimiyya) which is caused by the attribute of knowledge. (2) 'non-caused state' (al-hal ghayr al-mu'allal) – a state that is not grounded in an existent attribute, such as the blackness of black (sawadiyyat as-sawad). The proponents agree that all existents are equal in their essences (dhawat) and differ only in these states. Then Razi notes two positions on existence: the Ash'arite proponents of states among us (i.e., Sunni theologians) hold that existence is identical to the essence (nafs adh-dhat), while the Mu'tazila hold that existence is an additional attribute. The final sentence indicates that the argument for affirming that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is a thing (shay') is built upon this framework.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All states are caused by an additional attribute.
Correction: The classification shows that some states, like the blackness of black, are non-caused; they are essential properties not relying on a separate existent.

Checkpoint
In the classification of states, what is an example of a 'non-caused state' (al-hal ghayr al-mu'allal)?

B) The blackness of black (sawadiyyat as-sawad).
C) The state of existence.
D) The state of being an accident.

Key Takeaway
States are divided into those caused by an existent attribute and those not; existence itself is debated whether it is the essence or an attribute, affecting the status of the non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you often identify yourself by your attributes (knowledge, status, skills) rather than your very existence? How would your life change if you saw your essence as a gift from Allah, not defined by what you possess?
?
The Mu'tazila considered existence as an attribute, making the non-existent a thing. Have you ever treated something as real that has no true existence—like a fear or an imagined flaw? What would it mean to ground your reality only in what Allah has truly brought into being?
?
If your existence is not added to your essence but is your essence, then you cannot claim any part of your being as self-generated. How does this strip away your pride and force you to depend entirely on the Creator?

Why Necessity Is Not an External Attribute: Razi's Response
Razi responds to an objection that necessity is a mental relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's clarification that necessity in propositions is a mental modality, not an external attribute.

Core Idea
Necessity is a mental relation, not an extra thing in the world.

This lesson presents an objection to the idea that necessity is an additional attribute of the necessary being. The objector claims necessity is merely a mental relation. Razi responds by clarifying the true nature of necessity: it is a mental modality that depends on external realities, not an independent attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector says necessity and impossibility are modalities of the relation of subjects to predicates, so they are distinct and dependent.
Notice the objector assumes necessity is not a real attribute.
2
Understand Razi's diagnosis
Razi points out that the whole debate rests on the assumption that necessity is an existent attribute, which is false.
This is the key to understanding the response.
3
Grasp Razi's correction
Necessity is a mental modality that follows the relation, not a component of the subject or predicate.
It is a how of relation, not a thing.
4
Note the implication
The contingency of the mental modality does not imply that the external subject is contingent.
Mental categories do not dictate external reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمورض بأن الوجوب والامتناع كيفيات لانتساب الموضوعات إلى المحمولات فهى لا محالة مغارة ، للموضوعات والمحمولات وتابعة هما (1) (1) أقول : جميع ماقاله في الاستدلال والمعارضة مبنى على كون الوجوب أم أموجودا عارضا للواجب ، وقد مر بيان ما هو الحق فيه واعتبر ما أورده في المعارضة فإن وجوب القضايا لا يكون جزءاً من محمولاتها ولا من موضوعاتها بل يكون كيفية عقلية لانتساب محمو لانها إلى موضوعاتها، والكيفية العقلية لا تكون مستتبعة للأمور الخارجية بل تكون تابعا لها ، ولا يلزم من كونها في ذاتها ممكنة كون ما يتعلق به من الأمور الخارجية كنا . وفى عبارة صاحب الكتاب هو فإن الواجب أن يقول : كيفيتان لانتساب المحمولات إلى الموضوعات
He objects that necessity and impossibility are modalities of the relation of subjects to predicates, so they are necessarily distinct from the subjects and predicates and dependent on them. (1) I say: All that he said in the proof and objection is based on [the assumption] that necessity is an existent attribute of the necessary. The statement of what is true about it has passed. Consider what he presented in the objection: for necessity of propositions is not a part of their predicates nor of their subjects, but it is a mental modality of the relation of their predicates to their subjects. The mental modality does not necessitate external things but is dependent on them. And it does not follow from its being contingent in itself that what it pertains to from external things is … . And in the author's phrase, it is that the necessary should say: two modalities of the relation of predicates to subjects.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and Razi's response. The objector claims that necessity and impossibility are merely mental relations between subjects and predicates, not real attributes, so they are distinct from and dependent on the terms. Razi responds by pointing out that the whole debate rests on assuming necessity is an external attribute; but he clarifies that the necessity of propositions is a mental modality following the relation of predicate to subject. This mental modality is dependent on external realities, not the other way around. The contingency of the modality itself does not imply that the external subject is contingent.

Misconception: Necessity is an external attribute that exists independently in the necessary being.
Correction: Razi shows that necessity of propositions is a mental modality, not an external attribute; it depends on the relation of predicate to subject.

Checkpoint

A) An external attribute of the subject.
B) A mental modality dependent on the relation of predicate to subject.

Key Takeaway
Necessity in propositions is a mental modality dependent on external realities, not an additional attribute.

Refuting the Objection That Possibility Is Impossible
Razi presents and refutes an objection that possibility is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the objection that possibility is impossible, specifically how he demonstrates that the division of the possible into existent or nonexistent is incomplete.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental consideration, not a state of existence or nonexistence.

The concept of possibility (imkan) is foundational in kalam. Some object that if something is possible, it must be either existent or nonexistent, and in either case possibility is impossible. Razi responds by showing that this division is not exhaustive and that the essence can be considered without either state.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented: the possible must be either existent or nonexistent; in both cases possibility is negated. Also note the second formulation involving the cause's presence.
The objection aims to show that possibility leads to contradiction.
2
Examine Razi's first critique
Razi points out that the division into existence and nonexistence is not exhaustive. The essence (mahiyya) can be considered without either, so the objection fails.
This is a classic move: exposing a false dichotomy.
3
Examine Razi's second critique
He then criticizes the second formulation, showing that 'not present' is ambiguous and the division is incomplete.
This reinforces the idea that the objection relies on a flawed logical division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان معدوماً فهو حال العدم لا يقبل الوجود فلا يحصل إمكان الوجود والعدم ، وإذا استحال الخلو عن الوجود والعدم وكان كل واحد منهما منافيا للامكان كان القول بالإمكان محالا يمكن تقرير هذا السؤال من وجه آخر : وهو أن الممكن إما أن يكون قد حضر معه سبب وجوده أو لم يحضر ، وبالتقدير الأول يجب وبالتقدير الثانى يمتنع فيكون القول بالإمكان ممتنعا (1) أقول : القسمة فى قوله المحكوم عليه بالإمكان إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً ليست بحاصرة ، لان المفهوم منه أن المحكوم عليه بالإمكان إما أن يكون مع الوجود أو مع العدم ويعوزه قسم آخر وهو أن لا يكون مع أحدهما . وأما قوله : فإن كان موجوداً فهو حال الوجود لا يقبل العدم ، يقال له هذا مسلم ، أما في غير تلك الحال فلم لا يقبل الوجود وليس حال الماهية أما حال الوجود أو حال العدم، لأن هذين الحالين عند اعتبار الماهية مع الغير يمكن أن يقبل أحدهما لا بعينه ، وهذا الامتناع امتناع لاحق بشرط المحمول . وفي التقرير الثانى الذى قال لهيه إن الممكن إما أن يحضر معه سبب وجوده أو لم يحضر أيضاً فيه خلل ؛ لأن لم يحضر يحتمل أن يحضر معه لم يحضر سبب وجوده ، أو لم يحضر لا بسبب وجوده ، ولا لم يحضر سبب وجوده الذي هو سبب عدمه ، فظهر أن الخلل في هذا الكلام كان سبب أن القسمة لم تكن مستوفاة .
And if it is nonexistent, then in the state of nonexistence it cannot accept existence, so the possibility of existence and nonexistence does not occur. And if freedom from existence and nonexistence is impossible, and each of them is incompatible with possibility, then the assertion of possibility is impossible. This question can be formulated in another way: that the possible either has its cause of existence present with it or not. In the first case it is necessary, in the second it is impossible, so the assertion of possibility is impossible. (1) I say: The division in his statement 'what is predicated as possible is either existent or nonexistent' is not exhaustive, because what is understood from it is that what is predicated as possible is either with existence or with nonexistence, and it lacks another division: that it is not with either of them. And as for his statement 'if it is existent, then in the state of existence it cannot accept nonexistence', it is said to him: this is granted, but why should it not accept existence in a state other than that? And the state of the essence is not only the state of existence or the state of nonexistence, because these two states, when considering the essence with something else, it can accept one of them not specifically. And this impossibility is a consequent impossibility conditioned by the predicate. And in the second formulation which he mentioned: 'the possible either has its cause of existence present or not', there is also a flaw; because 'not present' may mean that its cause of existence is not present, or that it is not present without a cause of existence, nor not present without a cause of existence which is the cause of its nonexistence. So it is clear that the flaw in this statement is that the division is not complete.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the concept of possibility (imkan) and Razi's refutation. The objection argues that if a thing is possible, it must be either existent or nonexistent; if existent, it cannot accept nonexistence; if nonexistent, it cannot accept existence; thus possibility is impossible. A second formulation states that if the cause of existence is present, the possible is necessary; if absent, it is impossible; again possibility is impossible. Razi critiques the division as non-exhaustive, pointing out that the essence (mahiyya) can be considered without either existence or nonexistence, and the impossibility of change is only conditional. He also shows the flaw in the second formulation's dichotomy.

Key Terms
إمكان
ممكن
وجود
عدم
قسمة
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think possibility requires the possible thing to be either actually existent or nonexistent, leading to the conclusion that possibility is self-contradictory.
Correction: Razi shows that possibility is a rational judgment about the essence in itself, not requiring actual existence or nonexistence. The division into existence and nonexistence is not exhaustive, as the essence can be considered without either.

Checkpoint
How does Razi show the division in the first objection is incomplete?
A) By asserting that possibility can be both existence and nonexistence.
B) By pointing out that the essence can be considered without either existence or nonexistence.
C) By arguing that the possible must be necessary.
D) By denying the concept of possibility.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a rational judgment about the essence independent of actual existence or nonexistence; objections based on false dichotomies fail.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your future possibilities, do you treat them as fixed properties of the world, or do you recognize them as judgments in your mind? How does Razi's insight that possibility is a mental consideration about essences—not about actual states—change the weight you give to your own assessments of what is possible or impossible?
?
The objection in this lesson assumes that possibility must be grounded in either existence or nonexistence. Have you ever assumed that something is impossible simply because you cannot see how it could happen, forgetting that possibility is a judgment about the essence, not a fact about the current state? What would it mean for your trust in God's power to truly believe that possibility is not constrained by the present?
?
Razi shows that a faulty logical division leads to a false conclusion about reality. In your own life, are there areas where you hold an incomplete or false dichotomy—thinking there are only two options when a third exists? How might this lesson train you to examine your own assumptions before concluding something is impossible?
