The Seven Relational Accidents
Razi enumerates seven relational accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven types of accidents that imply relation according to Razi's classification.

Core Idea
Relational accidents describe how a substance relates to place, time, and other entities.

Razi begins his classification of accidents by dividing them into those that imply relation, those that imply division, and those that imply neither. This lesson focuses on the first category, which includes seven specific types that describe how a substance relates to place, time, other objects, and so on.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on relational accidents
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi lists seven types, each with a brief definition.
The passage starts with the overall division of accidents.
2
Identify each relational accident
Go through each of the seven types: al-ayn, al-mata, al-mudaf, al-milk, an yaf‘al, an yanfa‘il, al-wad‘. Understand their definitions.
These terms have specialized meanings in philosophy.
3
Reflect on examples
Think of concrete examples for each type, such as 'being in a room' for al-ayn or 'being a father' for mudaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العرض وهو إما أن يقتضي نسبة أو قسمة أو لا نسبة ولا قسمة، أما النسبة فسبعة أقسام . الأين وهو الحصول فى المكان والمتنى وهو الحصول فى الزمان أو في ظرفه، والمضاف وهو النسبة المتكررة، والملك ويقال له الجدة أيضا وهو كون الشيء محاطا بغيره الذي ينتقل بانتقاله وأن يفعل وهو التأثير وأن ينفعل وهو التأثر، والوضع وهو الهيئة الحاصلة للجسم بسبب ما بين أجزائه من النسب وما بين تلك الأجزاء وبين الأمور الخارجة عنها من النسب .
أما العرض الذي يقتضي القسمة فإما أن يكون بحيث ينقسم إلى أجزاء مشتركة في حد واحد وهو الحكم المتصل أو لا يشترك في حد واحد وهو الحكم المنفصل : أما المتصل فإما أن تكون الأجزاء المعترضة فيه بحيث توجد معا وإما أن لا يكون كذلك فالأول هو الكم المتصل القار الذات، وهو إما أن يكون ذا بعد واحد وهو الخط أو ذا بعدين وهو السطح أو ذا ثلاثة أبعاد وهو الجسم التعليمي. وأما الذي لا يكون قار الذات فهو الزمان فقط، وأما المنفصل فهو العدد، وأما العرض الذي لا يقتضي قسمة ولا نسبة فهو الكيف وأقسامه أربعة أحدها : المحسوسات بالحواس الخمس. وثانيها الكيفيات النفسانية . وثالثها التهيؤ إما للدفع وهو القوة أو للتأثر وهو اللا قوة ورابعها الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات، أما المتصلة كالاستقامة والانحناء وأما المنفصلة كالأولية والتركب والتقدم والآخر
As for the accident, it either implies relation, or division, or neither relation nor division. As for relation, there are seven types: (1) Where (al-ayn): existence in place. (2) When (al-mata): existence in time or in its container. (3) Relation (al-mudaf): repeated relation. (4) Possession (al-milk), also called al-jidda: a thing being surrounded by another that moves with it. (5) Acting (an yaf‘al): effecting. (6) Being acted upon (an yanfa‘il): being affected. (7) Position (al-wad‘): the configuration arising in a body due to the relations among its parts and between those parts and external matters.

As for the accident that implies division, it is either such that it divides into parts sharing one limit – the continuous quantity – or not sharing one limit – the discrete quantity. As for the continuous, either its intersecting parts exist together or not. The first is the continuous quantity with stable essence: either one-dimensional (line), two-dimensional (surface), or three-dimensional (teaching body). That which is not stable in essence is only time. The discrete is number. As for the accident that implies neither division nor relation, it is quality (al-kayf). Its divisions are four: (1) Sensibles by the five senses. (2) Psychic qualities. (3) Readiness, either for repulsion (power) or for being affected (non-power). (4) Qualities specific to quantities: continuous like straightness and curvature, discrete like primarity, composition, priority, and otherness.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the types of accidents according to the philosophers. It begins by dividing accidents into three categories: those implying relation (nisba), those implying division (qisma), and those implying neither (which is quality). The first category, relational accidents, includes seven types: where (position in space), when (position in time), relation (relative accident), possession (being surrounded by something that moves with it), acting (causality), being acted upon (passivity), and position (the configuration of a body's parts relative to each other and external things). The second category, division-implying accidents, is subdivided into continuous and discrete quantities. Continuous quantity is either fixed in essence (line, surface, three-dimensional body) or non-fixed (time). Discrete quantity is number. The third category, quality, has four types: sense-perceptible qualities, psychic qualities, readiness (power for repulsion or non-power for being affected), and qualities specific to quantities (continuous like straightness and curvature, discrete like primarity, composition, priority, and otherness). The passage provides a systematic taxonomy of accidents, essential for understanding Aristotelian physics as used in kalam.

Relational accidents include seven distinct types that describe how a substance relates to space, time, other substances, and its own parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The accident 'being acted upon' (an yanfa‘il) means you are constantly being affected by external forces. In your spiritual life, are you passive—merely reacting to circumstances—or are you actively 'acting' (an yaf‘al) for Allah's sake? Identify one area where you tend to be passive and commit to a single deliberate action today to change that.
?

What specific practice (like a daily dhikr or du‘a) can you begin right now to ensure your relation with Him is constant and living?

Quantitative Accidents: Continuous and Discrete
Razi subdivides quantity into continuous and discrete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi classifies quantities into continuous (with stable and non-stable essence) and discrete number.

Core Idea
Quantity divides into continuous (line, surface, body, time) and discrete (number).

The second category of accidents comprises those that imply division, i.e., quantities. Razi divides quantity into continuous and discrete, with further subdivisions. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for grasping how philosophers analyze extension and multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on quantitative accidents
Read the Arabic passage and translation focusing on the part about division-implying accidents.
Note the initial division into continuous and discrete.
2
Trace the subdivisions of continuous quantity
Identify the three types of continuous quantity with stable essence (line, surface, volume) and the one non-stable (time).
'Teaching body' is mathematical body.
3
Understand discrete quantity
Note that discrete quantity is simply number.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Time is a continuous quantity whose parts do not coexist. You cannot return to a single moment—each 'now' is shared only as a limit. How does this truth expose the urgency of your repentance? What sin have you been postponing because you assume the next moment is yours to hold?
?
Number is discrete: you count your breaths, your deeds, your days. Your Lord counts everything precisely. If every single act of yours is numbered, and not a single one is lost, how will you face the record of your life? What deed today will you count that you will be glad to see on your scale?
?
Your physical body is a substance, but the 'teaching body' is only an accident—a shape. If your value lies not in your dimensions but in what is beyond them, why do you still cling to the fleeting measurements of status and appearance? What would it mean to measure yourself only by what pleases Allah?

The Four Types of Quality
Razi enumerates four types of quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of qualities according to Razi's classification.

Core Idea
Quality includes sensible, psychic, readiness, and quantity-specific qualities.

After covering relational and quantitative accidents, Razi turns to quality, which implies neither relation nor division. He lists four kinds: sensible qualities, psychic qualities, readiness (power or non-power), and qualities specific to quantities. This lesson unpacks each category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on quality
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the quality section.
Quality is the third main category.
2
Identify the four types
List the four: sensible, psychic, readiness, and quantity-specific. Note the subdivisions.
Readiness includes power and non-power.
3
Understand quantity-specific qualities
Note that these are further divided into continuous (e.g., straightness) and discrete (e.g., primarity).

The Five Categories of Substance in Islamic Kalam
Imam Razi classifies substance into five kinds: form, matter, body, soul, and intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of substance (jawhar) and its five subcategories as classified by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Substance is not only physical body; it includes form, matter, soul, and intellect.

Imam Razi presents a systematic classification of all possible entities. Starting from the broad division into accident and substance, he then divides substance into five types based on their relationship to a substrate. This passage establishes the foundational categories used in kalam to discuss the nature of created beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial division
First, note the broad division: the possible entity is either in a subject (accident) or not (substance). Understand this binary.
2
Identify the five substance types
Imam Razi lists five kinds of substance: form, matter, body, soul, and intellect. Find each description in the text.
Body is said to be 'by induction' (bil-istiqra').
3
Understand the defining relations
Each type is defined by its relation to a substrate (mahall): form is in a substrate, matter is a substrate, body is composite, soul governs bodies, intellect is completely separate.
This shows that substance includes both material and immaterial entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الممكن إما أن يكون فى الموضوع وهو العرض ، أولا يكون وهو الجوهر ، والجوهر إما أن يكون في المحل وهو الصورة أو يكون محلا وهو الهيولى أو مركبا من الصورة والهيولي وهو الجسم فقط بالاستقراء أو لا حالا ولا محلا ولا مركبا منهما، وهو إما أن يكون متعلقا بالأجسام تعلق التدبير وهو النفس أولا يكون وهو العقل
If you have known this, we say: The possible entity is either in a subject, and it is the accident, or it is not, and it is the substance. The substance is either in a substrate, and it is the form; or it is a substrate, and it is the matter (hayula); or it is composed of form and matter, and it is the body (jism) only by induction; or it is neither a substrate nor a substrate's occupant nor composed of them, and it is either related to bodies with a relation of management, and it is the soul (nafs); or it is not, and it is the intellect ('aql).
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the two fundamental categories of contingent existence: substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad). Imam Razi begins with the possible entity (al-mumkin). If it exists in a subject, it is an accident; if not, it is a substance. Then he divides substance into five kinds based on its relation to a substrate (mahall): form (sura) which is in a substrate; matter (hayula) which is a substrate; body (jism) which is composed of both form and matter; soul (nafs) which is related to bodies by way of governance; and intellect ('aql) which is completely separate. This classification is standard in the Peripatetic tradition, and Razi notes that the identification of body as composed of form and matter is by induction (bil-istiqra').

Key Terms
الممكن
العرض
الجوهر
الصورة
الهيولى
الجسم
النفس
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Substance (jawhar) refers only to physical bodies.
Correction: Substance includes immaterial entities like soul and intellect, as per this classification.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a type of substance (jawhar)?
A) Form (sura)
B) Matter (hayula)
C) Accident ('arad)
D) Soul (nafs)

Key Takeaway
The classification of substance into five types—form, matter, body, soul, and intellect—establishes the building blocks for discussing all contingent beings in Islamic philosophy and kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a soul that manages your body. In the past week, have you been feeding your soul with what truly sustains it, or have you let your body's desires dominate? What will you do today to let your soul lead?
?
Your intellect is a separate substance, not bound to the physical. When was the last time you used it to ponder a deep truth beyond daily tasks? Commit to setting aside 10 minutes today for silent reflection on a theological truth from this lesson.
?
If you truly believed that your soul and intellect are real substances, how would your priorities change? Write one specific action you will take in the next 24 hours to honor your soul and intellect over your body.

The Threefold Classification and Relational Accidents
Razi divides accidents into three categories and lists seven relational accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's threefold division of accidents and be able to name and define the seven relational accidents.

Core Idea
Accidents are either relational, divisional, or neither—seven types cover relation.

Imam Razi opens his classification by dividing all accidents into three broad categories. The first category, relational accidents, includes seven distinct kinds that map the ways things can be related to space, time, possession, action, passion, and position. This system provides a complete inventory of relational modes in the created world.

Guided Path
1
Read the threefold division
Start with the opening sentence that divides accidents into three categories: those entailing relation, division, or neither. Notice the logical structure.
This threefold division is the foundation for the entire classification.
2
Learn the seven relational accidents
Go through each of the seven kinds: ayn, mata, mudaf, milk, an yaf'ala, an yanfa'ila, and wad'. Focus on how each is defined by a specific type of relation.
These seven are the complete set of relational accidents.
3
Reflect on the cases
Think of concrete examples for each relational accident, such as 'the book on the table' for ayn, 'yesterday' for mata, and so on.
This will help internalize the categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العرض وهو إما أن يقتض نسبة أو قسمة أولا نسبة ولا قسمة . أما النسبة فسبعة أقسام . الأين وهو الحصول فى المكان والمتنى وهو الحصول فى الزمان أو في ظرفه ، والمضاف وهو النسبة المتكررة ، والملك ويقال له الجدة أيضا وهو كون الشىء محاطا بغيره الدى ينتقل بانتقاله وأن يفعل وهو التأثير وأن ينفعل وهو التأثر ، والوضع وهو الهيئة الحاصلة للجسم بسبب ما بين أجزائه من النسب وما بين تلك الأجزاء وبين الأمور الخارجة عنها من النسب . أما العرض الذى يقتضى القسمة فإما أن يكون بحيث ينقسم إلى أجزاء مشتركة في حد واحد وهو الحكم المتصل أو لا يشترك في حد واحد وهو الحكم المنفصل : أما المتصل فإما أن تكون الأجزاء المعترضة فيه بحيث توجد معا وإما أن لا يكون كذلك فالأول هو الكم المتصل القار الذات ، وهو إما أن يكون ذا بعد واحد وهو الخط أو ذا بعدين وهو السطح أوذا ثلاثة أبعاد وهو الجسم التعليمي. وأما الذى لا يكون قار الذات فهو الزمان فقط ، وأما المنفصل فهو العدد ، وأما العرض الذى لا يقتضى قسمة ولا نسبة فهو الكيف وأقسامه أربعة أحدها : المحسوسات بالحواس الخمس. وثانيها الكيفيات النفسانية . وثالثها التهيؤ إما للدفع وهو القوة أو للتأثر وهو اللا قوة ورابعها الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات، أما المتصلة كالاستقامة والانحناء وأما المنفصلة كالأولية والتركب والتقدم والآخر . معقول عندهم ، والمراد مهنا من الحال الذى يكون سببا لقوام المحل ههنا هو الصورة ومن المحل الهيولى، ويريدون بهذا البيان أن امتناع الانفكاك بينهما لاحتياج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر لا يقتضى الدير والحال الذى لا يتقوم به محله هو العرض ومحله الموضوع
As for the accident, it either entails a relation or a division, or neither relation nor division. As for relation, it has seven kinds: 'ayn (location in place), mata (location in time or its container), mudaf (repeated relation), milk (ownership, also called jidda), which is a thing being surrounded by another that transfers with its transfer; 'an yaf'ala (to act, which is influence); 'an yanfa'ila (to be acted upon, which is being influenced); and wad' (position), which is the state acquired by the body due to the relations among its parts and the relations between those parts and external things. As for the accident that entails division, it is either such that it divides into parts sharing a single limit—this is the continuous (muttasil) judgment—or not sharing a single limit—this is the discrete (munfasil) judgment. The continuous is either such that the intercepted parts exist together or not. The first is the continuous quantity that is stationary in essence (qar al-dhat), and it is either one-dimensional (line), two-dimensional (surface), or three-dimensional (the educational body). That which is not stationary in essence is time only. As for the discrete (quantity), it is number. As for the accident that entails neither division nor relation, it is quality (kayf). Its divisions are four: first, sensibles by the five senses; second, psychological qualities; third, disposition (tahayyu') either for repulsion (quwwa) or for being affected (la quwwa); fourth, qualities specific to quantities: continuous ones like straightness and curvature, discrete ones like priority, composition, anteriority, and posteriority. This is understood by them. What is meant here by 'the state (hal) that is a cause for the subsistence of the substrate' is the form? and by 'substrate' is matter. They intend by this explanation that the impossibility of separation between them due to each needing the other does not necessitate that one is a state and the other a substrate? And the state that does not subsist by its substrate is the accident, and its substrate is the subject.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of accidents. Imam Razi begins by dividing accidents into three broad categories: those entailing a relation (nisba), those entailing division (qisma), and those entailing neither (kayf). The first category includes seven 'relational' categories: location in place (ayn), location in time (mata), relation (idafa), possession (milk), action (an yaf'ala), passion (an yanfa'ila), and position (wad'). The second category includes quantity, which is either continuous (muttasil) or discrete (munfasil). Continuous quantity is either stationary (like line, surface, volume) or non-stationary (time). Discrete quantity is number. The third category is quality, which has four types: sensible qualities, psychological qualities, dispositions (for resistance or susceptibility), and qualities specific to quantity (like straightness, curvature, priority). After this enumeration, Razi adds a clarification about the terms 'hal' (state) and 'mahall' (substrate), explaining that in this context, 'state' refers to form, 'substrate' to matter, and that their inseparability does not imply they are the same; rather, the accident is that which does not subsist by its substrate.

Key Takeaway
Razi starts his classification of accidents by identifying seven distinct types of relational accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the seven relational accidents do you think most accurately describes your relationship with your possessions? How does Razi's definition of 'milk' (being surrounded by what moves with you) challenge your attachment to material things that cannot accompany you to the Hereafter?
?
Razi distinguishes between 'to act' (an yaf'ala) and 'to be acted upon' (an yanfa'ila). In which of your daily interactions do you find yourself more often as the one influencing, rather than being influenced? How does this awareness shape your responsibility before Allah for the effects you have on others?
?
The accident of 'position' (wad') arises from relations among parts and external things. Reflect on your own physical posture and surroundings. In what ways does your environment shape your spiritual state, and what concrete change can you make today to align your position with greater mindfulness of Allah?

Accidents Entailing Division: Quantity
Razi classifies accidents entailing division into continuous and discrete quantity, with subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi subdivides accidents of division into continuous and discrete quantity, including their further breakdown.

Core Idea
Division-based accidents yield quantity: continuous (stationary or temporal) and discrete (number).

After relational accidents, Razi turns to those that entail division. He distinguishes between continuous and discrete quantity, and further divides continuous quantity into stationary (line, surface, volume) and non-stationary (time). Discrete quantity is simply number. This taxonomy forms the basis for understanding quantitative categories in the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types: continuous vs. discrete
Read the opening lines that divide division-based accidents into those where parts share a single limit (continuous) and those where they do not (discrete).
This mirrors the distinction between continuous and discrete in geometry and arithmetic.
2
Explore continuous quantity subdivisions
Continuous is further split into stationary (qar al-dhat) and non-stationary (time). Stationary continuous has dimensions: line, surface, volume.
Note that time is considered a continuous quantity but not stationary.
3
Identify discrete quantity
Discrete quantity is simply number, which does not admit of a shared limit between its parts.
Numbers are discrete because each unit is separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Accidents Entailing Neither: Quality (Kayf)
Razi defines quality and lists its four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of quality as an accident without relation or division, and you will know its four types.

Core Idea
Quality is the catch-all for accidents that are neither relational nor divisional, with four distinct kinds.

The third and final category of accidents is quality (kayf), which has no relation or division. Razi lists four types: sensible qualities, psychological qualities, dispositions (for power or impotence), and qualities specific to quantity. This completes the classification of all accidents and highlights the breadth of qualitative attributes in the world.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of quality
Read the opening line: quality is what does not entail relation or division.
This is a residual category.
2
Memorize the four types
Go through each: (1) sensible qualities by the five senses, (2) psychological qualities, (3) dispositions (tahayyu') either for repulsion (quwwa) or affection (la quwwa), (4) qualities specific to quantities (continuous and discrete).
The fourth type includes examples like straightness and priority.
3
Reflect on examples
Think of examples for each type: redness (sensible), joy (psychological), strength (disposition), curvature (quantity-specific).
This helps solidify the categories.

Clarification on State (Hal) and Substrate (Mahall)
Razi clarifies the terms 'hal' and 'mahall' regarding form and matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's clarification of the terms 'hal' (state) and 'mahall' (substrate) in the context of form and matter, and the nature of accidents.

Core Idea
The inseparability of form and matter does not make form a state; accidents are what do not subsist by their substrate.

After completing the classification of accidents, Razi adds a philosophical clarification. He explains that in this context, 'state' (hal) refers to form, and 'substrate' (mahall) refers to matter. Their mutual inseparability does not imply they are the same; rather, an accident is precisely that which does not subsist by its substrate. This distinction refines the understanding of substance and accident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Read the passage and note the terms 'hal' (state) and 'mahall' (substrate). Understand that here 'hal' means form (sura) and 'mahall' means matter (hayula).
This usage is specific to the philosophical context.
2
Understand the argument
Razi argues that the impossibility of separation between form and matter does not entail that form is a state and matter is a substrate. Rather, the accident is what does not subsist by its substrate.
This clarifies the definition of accident.
3
Apply the clarification
Reflect on how this affects the earlier classification: accidents are those that inhere in a substrate but are not essential to it.
This is a foundational point for kalam ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العرض وهو إما أن يقتض نسبة أو قسمة أولا نسبة ولا قسمة . أما النسبة فسبعة أقسام . الأين وهو الحصول فى المكان والمتنى وهو الحصول فى الزمان أو في ظرفه ، والمضاف وهو النسبة المتكررة ، والملك ويقال له الجدة أيضا وهو كون الشىء محاطا بغيره الدى ينتقل بانتقاله وأن يفعل وهو التأثير وأن ينفعل وهو التأثر ، والوضع وهو الهيئة الحاصلة للجسم بسبب ما بين أجزائه من النسب وما بين تلك الأجزاء وبين الأمور الخارجة عنها من النسب . أما العرض الذى يقتضى القسمة فإما أن يكون بحيث ينقسم إلى أجزاء مشتركة في حد واحد وهو الحكم المتصل أو لا يشترك في حد واحد وهو الحكم المنفصل : أما المتصل فإما أن تكون الأجزاء المعترضة فيه بحيث توجد معا وإما أن لا يكون كذلك فالأول هو الكم المتصل القار الذات ، وهو إما أن يكون ذا بعد واحد وهو الخط أو ذا بعدين وهو السطح أوذا ثلاثة أبعاد وهو الجسم التعليمي. وأما الذى لا يكون قار الذات فهو الزمان فقط ، وأما المنفصل فهو العدد ، وأما العرض الذى لا يقتضى قسمة ولا نسبة فهو الكيف وأقسامه أربعة أحدها : المحسوسات بالحواس الخمس. وثانيها الكيفيات النفسانية . وثالثها التهيؤ إما للدفع وهو القوة أو للتأثر وهو اللا قوة ورابعها الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات، أما المتصلة كالاستقامة والانحناء وأما المنفصلة كالأولية والتركب والتقدم والآخر . معقول عندهم ، والمراد مهنا من الحال الذى يكون سببا لقوام المحل ههنا هو الصورة ومن المحل الهيولى، ويريدون بهذا البيان أن امتناع الانفكاك بينهما لاحتياج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر لا يقتضى الدير والحال الذى لا يتقوم به محله هو العرض ومحله الموضوع
As for the accident, it either entails a relation or a division, or neither relation nor division. As for relation, it has seven kinds: 'ayn (location in place), mata (location in time or its container), mudaf (repeated relation), milk (ownership, also called jidda), which is a thing being surrounded by another that transfers with its transfer; 'an yaf'ala (to act, which is influence); 'an yanfa'ila (to be acted upon, which is being influenced); and wad' (position), which is the state acquired by the body due to the relations among its parts and the relations between those parts and external things. As for the accident that entails division, it is either such that it divides into parts sharing a single limit—this is the continuous (muttasil) judgment—or not sharing a single limit—this is the discrete (munfasil) judgment. The continuous is either such that the intercepted parts exist together or not. The first is the continuous quantity that is stationary in essence (qar al-dhat), and it is either one-dimensional (line), two-dimensional (surface), or three-dimensional (the educational body). That which is not stationary in essence is time only. As for the discrete (quantity), it is number. As for the accident that entails neither division nor relation, it is quality (kayf). Its divisions are four: first, sensibles by the five senses; second, psychological qualities; third, disposition (tahayyu') either for repulsion (quwwa) or for being affected (la quwwa); fourth, qualities specific to quantities: continuous ones like straightness and curvature, discrete ones like priority, composition, anteriority, and posteriority. This is understood by them. What is meant here by 'the state (hal) that is a cause for the subsistence of the substrate' is the form? and by 'substrate' is matter. They intend by this explanation that the impossibility of separation between them due to each needing the other does not necessitate that one is a state and the other a substrate? And the state that does not subsist by its substrate is the accident, and its substrate is the subject.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of accidents. Imam Razi begins by dividing accidents into three broad categories: those entailing a relation (nisba), those entailing division (qisma), and those entailing neither (kayf). The first category includes seven 'relational' categories: location in place (ayn), location in time (mata), relation (idafa), possession (milk), action (an yaf'ala), passion (an yanfa'ila), and position (wad'). The second category includes quantity, which is either continuous (muttasil) or discrete (munfasil). Continuous quantity is either stationary (like line, surface, volume) or non-stationary (time). Discrete quantity is number. The third category is quality, which has four types: sensible qualities, psychological qualities, dispositions (for resistance or susceptibility), and qualities specific to quantity (like straightness, curvature, priority). After this enumeration, Razi adds a clarification about the terms 'hal' (state) and 'mahall' (substrate), explaining that in this context, 'state' refers to form, 'substrate' to matter, and that their inseparability does not imply they are the same; rather, the accident is that which does not subsist by its substrate.

المحل
الصورة
الهيولى
الانفكاك
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that form and matter are like accident and substance because they are inseparable.
Correction: Razi clarifies that form and matter are not related as accident and substrate; rather, the accident is defined by its lack of self-subsistence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's clarification, what is the accident?
A) That which subsists by itself
B) That which does not subsist by its substrate
C) Form that gives subsistence to matter
D) Matter that receives form

Key Takeaway
Razi distinguishes the form-matter relation from the accident-substrate relation, defining the accident as that which does not subsist by its substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage defines an accident as that which cannot subsist by itself. In your own life, what do you most depend on for your sense of self? Is it something essential or accidental? Will you examine this dependency today?
?
Razi corrects a misconception about the term 'hal'. Are there any terms in your understanding of Islam that you may have misunderstood or taken for granted? Commit to researching one such term this week.
?
If form and matter are inseparable yet distinct, what does that teach you about how you perceive the relationship between your body and soul? How can this understanding change how you treat your body?

Why the Mutakallimun Deny Relational Accidents
The mutakallimun argue against the reality of relational accidents by showing they lead to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's arguments against the existence of relational accidents, particularly the problems of infinite regress and circularity.

Core Idea
Relational accidents lead to infinite regress, so they cannot be real.

The mutakallimun (early Muslim theologians) argued that relations, simultaneity, influence, and reception cannot be real attributes inhering in substances. Their reasoning hinges on the absurdity of an infinite regress of relations. This lesson walks through their key arguments against relational accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the arguments come through.
2
Identify the five relational concepts
The passage discusses five kinds of relational accidents: relation (idafa), simultaneity (ma'iyya), temporal relation (nisba ila al-zaman), influence (ta'thir), and reception (qabul). Notice each is treated separately.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
For each type, the mutakallimun claim that if it were a real attribute, an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow. Trace the reasoning for one example, such as idafa.
The key is that the inherence of a relation would itself be a relation, leading to an endless chain.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Ask why this mattered: preserving God's immutability (by denying real change in God) and avoiding circular definitions of existence. The arguments protect tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المتكلمون فقد أنكروا وجود الأعراض النسبية أما الإضافة فلأنها لو كانت موجودة لكانت في محل وحلولها في محلها نسبة بين ذاتها وبين ذلك المحل فكانت غير ذاتها وذلك الغير أيضاً يكون حالا في المحل فيكون حلوله زائدا ولزم التسلسل ، ولأن كل حادث يحدث فإن الله تعالى يكون موجودا معه في ذلك الزمان، فلو كانت تلك المعية صفة وجودية ازم حدوث الصفة في ذات الله تعالى ، ولان الإضافة لو كانت صفة موجودة لمكان وجودها غير ماهيتها بناء على أن الوجود وصف مشترك فيه بين كل الموجودات ، فصول وجودها لماهيتها إضافة بين وجودها وماهيتها وتلك الإضافة سابقة على تحقيق الإضافة الموجودة فيكون الشيء موجودا قبل نفسه هذا خلف وأما نسبة الشيء إلى الزمان فلو كانت صفة وجودية لسكان لها نسبة أخرى إلى ذلك الزمان و لزم التسلسل ، وكذا التأثير لو كان صفة زائدة لكانت تلك الصفة ممكنة بذاتها مفتقرة إلى مؤثر وكان تأثير المؤثر فيها صفة أخرى ولزم التسلسل ، وكذا القبول لو كان صفة زائدة لكانت موصوفية الذات بها صفة أخرى و لزم التسلسل
As for the mutakallimun, they denied the existence of relational accidents. As for relation (idafa), because if it were existent, it would be in a substrate, and its indwelling in its substrate would be a relation between itself and that substrate, so it would be other than itself, and that other would also be an indweller in the substrate, so its indwelling would be additional, and an infinite regress would follow. Also because every event that occurs, God exists with it at that time. If that 'withness' (ma'iyya) were an existential attribute, it would necessitate the occurrence of an attribute in God's essence. Also because if relation were an existent attribute, its existence would be other than its quiddity, on the basis that existence is a common predicate among all existents. So the existence's being added to its quiddity would be a relation between its existence and its quiddity, and that relation would be prior to the realization of the existent relation, so the thing would be existent before itself—this is a contradiction. As for the relation of a thing to time, if it were an existential attribute, it would have another relation to that time, and infinite regress would follow. Likewise, influence (ta'thir) if it were an additional attribute, that attribute would be possible in itself, needing an influencer, and the influence of the influencer upon it would be another attribute, and infinite regress would follow. Likewise, reception (qabul) if it were an additional attribute, the essence's being described by it would be another attribute, and infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's arguments against the reality of relational accidents (al-a'rad al-nisabiyya). They argue that if relations were real attributes inhering in subjects, they would lead to contradictions and infinite regress. The first argument concerns the category of relation (idafa): if a relation existed in a subject, its inherence would itself be a relation, leading to an infinite chain. The second argument concerns God's simultaneous existence with events: if that simultaneity (ma'iyya) were a real attribute, it would entail a change in God's essence. The third argument: if relation were an attribute distinct from its essence, then the relation between existence and essence would be prior to the relation itself, causing a vicious circle. Similar arguments are applied to temporal relations (nisba ila al-zaman), influence (ta'thir), and reception (qabul), all leading to infinite regress. These arguments reflect the Ash'ari rejection of accidents that are purely relational, as they consider only intrinsic qualities as real.

Key Terms
الأعراض النسبية
الإضافة
المعية
التأثير
القبول
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun deny all relations, including mental or conceptual relations.
Correction: They only deny that relations are extra-mental, real attributes inhering in substances. Relations as mental considerations are accepted.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence the mutakallimun claim follows from treating relation (idafa) as an existent attribute?
A) It would multiply gods.
B) It would lead to infinite regress (tasalsul).
C) It would make God corporeal.
D) It would negate free will.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's rejection of relational accidents is based on the logical impossibility of an infinite regress that would follow if they were real attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say 'God is with me'—but if that 'withness' were a real attribute, it would imply a change in God. Do you truly believe God is unchanging, or do you secretly imagine a God who enters into new relations with you? How does this lesson force you to purify your concept of God?
?
The mutakallimun denied that influence (ta'thir) is a real attribute. When you act, do you attribute the actual influence of your actions to yourself or to God? Are you willing to admit that your own causal power is merely a mental consideration, and that true influence belongs only to God?
?
If relations are not real entities, then your perception of distance from God—near or far—is not a real accident but a mental construct. Does that comfort you or disturb you? What does it mean for your spiritual striving to 'draw near' to God if nearness is not a real relation?

The Philosophers' Proof for Relational Attributes
The philosophers present a proof for the reality of relational accidents based on coming-into-being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that relational attributes like 'aboveness' are real positive entities, not mere mental constructs.

Core Idea
Relational attributes must be real because they can come into existence after non-existence.

The philosophers challenge the mutakallimun's view that relations are mental constructs by pointing to change: something can go from not-above to above. This change requires the new attribute to be a real, positive thing. Let's examine their proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the philosophers' argument in Arabic and English. Note how they start with the example of heaven being above earth.
2
Identify the two key premises
The proof relies on: (1) the relational state exists independently of mental consideration, and (2) change from non-existence to existence implies reality.
3
Understand the impossibility of non-existential
If 'aboveness' were non-existential, its coming to be would be negation of negation, which is impossible. Therefore it must be positive.
This is a form of argument from change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحكماء : فقد احتجوا على ثبوت هذه النسب بأن كون السماء فرق الأرض مثلا أمر حاصل سواء وجد الفرض والاعتبار أو لم يوجد وهو ليس أمرا عدمها لأن الشىء قد لا يكون فوقا ثم يصير فوقا ، فالفوقية التي حصلت بعد العدم لا تكون عدمية وإلا لكان نفى النفى عدميا وهو محال ، فالفوقية أمر ثبوتى وليست أقول : لو كانت هذه المقولات نسبا لكانت أنواعا لجنس عال هو النسبة ولم تكن أجناسا عالية ، وهم لا يعنون بها ما يدخل النسبة فى ذاتها بل بما يعرض لها النسب إلا الإضافة ، فإن مفهومها النسبة ويستدعى تحكوار النسبة ، وأما كون الإضافة عرضا حالا في محل فحلولها فى ذلك المحل لا يكون إضافة ، بل الإضافة تفرض للحال إلى المحل والمحل إلى الحال بعد الحلول ، كما تفرض للرأس ولدى الرأس والتحقيق ههنا أن وجود الإضافة الحقيقية لا يكون إلا فى العقل ولا يكون فى الخارج الا كون الموجود بحيث يحدث في العقل من تصوره الإضافة ، فإن ولادة شخص من شخص أمر موجود في الخارج ، وإذا تصوره العاقل يعقل أبوة في أحدهما وبنوة في الآخر ولا يلزم التسلسل ، لأن الأبوة إذا عرضت لشخص وإن كان ذلك المروض إضافة أخرى لكنها لا تكون بأبوة أخرى فإذا لا تتسلسل الأبوة وتلك الإضافة أيضاً أمر عقلى ولا تتسلسل، لأنها تنقطع عند وقوف العقل ، وهم يقولون إن لله تعالى صفات إضافية كالأول والآخر والخالق والرازق والمبدع والصانع وغير ذلك ، ويلتزمون القول بهذه الصفات غير المعية الزمانية لله تعالى وأما قوله : حصول الوجود الماهية إضافة بينهما فليس بشيء لأن الإضافة مهنا ليست إلا بمعنى الانضمام وليس ذلك ما نحن فيه ، وكون الشىء فى الزمان نسبية كون الجسم في المكان الذى يقول بوجوده المتكلم ، وأما النسبة فيلحقها بعد ثبوتها، وأما التأثير فليس كل تأثير من هذه المقولة بل يريدون التأثير الصادر عن المؤثر في زمان غير قار الذات كقطع السكين اللحم ، فإن الجزءين لا يقعان في زمان واحد ، فالهيئة الحاصلة للسكين حين يقال له هوذا يقطع لا قبله ولا بعده هي المعنية بأن يفعل ، وقس عليه الانفعال. والنسبة إنما تعرض للعقل بين القاطع والمقطوع والإنصاف يقتضى أن ينقل مذاهب الخصوم على ماذهبوا لئلا يلحق الناقلين شناعة بسبب سوء العقل
As for the philosophers, they adduced proof for the existence of these relations: that the heaven being above the earth, for example, is a matter obtained whether assumption and consideration exist or not, and it is not a matter of non-existence, because a thing may not be above and then becomes above. So the 'aboveness' (fawqiyya) that occurs after non-existence cannot be non-existential, otherwise the negation of negation would be non-existential, which is impossible. So 'aboveness' is a positive thing and not... I say: If these categories were relations, they would be species of a higher genus which is relation, and would not be highest genera. They (the philosophers) do not mean by them that relation enters into their essence, but rather that relation accidentally attaches to them, except for 'idafa', for its concept is relation and it requires the determination of relation. As for 'idafa' being an accident inhering in a substrate, its inherence in that substrate is not a relation; rather, relation is supposed between the state and the substrate, and between the substrate and the state after inherence, just as it is supposed between the head and what is at the head. The verification here is that the existence of a real relation is only in the mind, and in the external world there is only the existent being such that from its conception in the mind a relation arises. For example, the birth of a person from a person is a matter existing externally. When the rational person conceives it, he conceives paternity in one and sonship in the other, and infinite regress does not follow, because when paternity attaches to a person, even if that subject of relation is another relation, it is not by another paternity; thus paternity does not regress infinitely, and that relation is a mental thing, and it does not regress because it stops at the mind's stop. They (the philosophers) say that God has relational attributes like 'the First', 'the Last', 'the Creator', 'the Provider', 'the Originator', 'the Maker', and others, and they affirm these attributes, except for temporal 'withness' (ma'iyya) for God. As for his statement: 'the obtaining of existence to quiddity is a relation between them', it is nothing, because 'relation' here is only in the sense of conjunction (indimam), not what we are discussing. And a thing's being in time is relative like a body's being in place, as the mutakallim says exists. As for relation, it attaches to it after its existence. As for influence (ta'thir), not every influence is of this category; rather, they mean the influence emanating from the influencer in non-stationary time, like the cutting of meat by a knife, for the two parts do not occur in one time. So the state acquired by the knife when it is said 'it is cutting'—neither before nor after—is what is meant by 'to act'. Measure upon it passion (infi'al). Relation only occurs in the mind between the cutter and the cut. Fairness requires that the doctrines of the opponents be transmitted as they held, so that transmitters not be disgraced due to poor understanding.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: first, the philosophers' proof for the reality of relational accidents, and second, the editor's extensive clarification and refutation of the mutakallimun's arguments. The philosophers argue that 'aboveness' (fawqiyya) is a real attribute because it can come into existence after not existing; otherwise, the negation of negation would be impossible. The editor then intervenes to explain that the categories of relation are not themselves relations in their essence, except for 'idafa' which is purely relational. He clarifies that inherence of an accident in a substrate is not itself a relation; rather, relation is a mental consideration after inherence. Real relation exists only in the mind, not externally; external things only give rise to mental relations. The example of paternity and sonship illustrates that no infinite regress occurs because these are mental relations that stop at the mind. He also addresses specific objections: God's relational attributes (like Creator) are affirmed; the relation between existence and quiddity is mere conjunction, not the relational category; temporal location is analogous to spatial location; and influence (ta'thir) is a special case of non-stationary action. He concludes with a methodological note about faithfully presenting opponents' views.

Key Terms
الحكماء
الفوقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relational attributes are merely mental constructs with no external reality.
Correction: The philosophers argue that relational attributes must have some external reality because they can come into existence after non-existence, which is a change that requires a real attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, why can't 'aboveness' be a non-existential?
A) Because non-existentials cannot change.
B) Because its coming into being would be the negation of negation, which is impossible.
C) Because it is always present.
D) Because the mutakallimun said so.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers prove that relational attributes like 'aboveness' are real positive entities because they can come into existence after not existing, which cannot be explained by mere non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of a relationship you have—like being a parent, a spouse, or a leader—do you treat it as a real entity that Allah sees and judges, or just a label that could change with perception? How would your daily actions change if you truly believed that every relational state is a real, created thing that will be accounted for?
?
Think of a time a relationship in your life changed—like becoming a friend or a colleague. Did you consider the change in the relationship as merely a change in how you think about it, or did you recognize it as a real, ontologically significant event before Allah? If relational attributes are real and come into being, what does that mean for the way you maintain and honor each relationship?
?
If every 'non-existence' of a relation turning into existence is a real positive entity created by Allah, then every new bond or role you enter into is a direct gift from Him. Are there any relationships in your life that you have neglected to thank Allah for, or that you have allowed to become 'non-existent' through sin? What concrete step will you take today to affirm the reality of a key relationship—by giving it the time, respect, and intention it deserves as a real attribute of your existence?

The Editor's Clarification: Relation as Mental Construct
The editor clarifies the philosophers' position on the mental existence of relations and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's explanation that real relations exist only in the mind, and how he addresses objections regarding infinite regress, God's attributes, and the category of influence.

Core Idea
Real relations are mental constructs grounded in external realities, not independently existing accidents.

The editor responds to the mutakallimun's critique by clarifying what the philosophers actually mean by relational attributes. He explains that the only truly relational category is 'idafa' (relation), and even that exists only in the mind. He then tackles specific issues like infinite regress, God's relational names, and the nature of temporal action.

Guided Path
1
Read the editor's clarification
Read the passage from 'I say' onward. Note the distinction between inherence and relation.
2
Examine the paternity example
See how the editor uses the example of paternity and sonship to illustrate that relation is mental and avoids infinite regress.
3
Understand the applications
Notice how the editor applies the same principle to God's attributes, the existence-quiddity relation, temporal location, and influence.

أما الحكماء : فقد احتجوا على ثبوت هذه النسب بأن كون السماء فرق الأرض مثلا أمر حاصل سواء وجد الفرض والاعتبار أو لم يوجد وهو ليس أمرا عدمها لأن الشىء قد لا يكون فوقا ثم يصير فوقا ، فالفوقية التي حصلت بعد العدم لا تكون عدمية وإلا لكان نفى النفى عدميا وهو محال ، فالفوقية أمر ثبوتى وليست أقول : لو كانت هذه المقولات نسبا لكانت أنواعا لجنس عال هو النسبة ولم تكن أجناسا عالية ، وهم لا يعنون بها ما يدخل النسبة فى ذاتها بل بما يعرض لها النسب إلا الإضافة ، فإن مفهومها النسبة ويستدعى تحكوار النسبة ، وأما كون الإضافة عرضا حالا في محل فحلولها فى ذلك المحل لا يكون إضافة ، بل الإضافة تفرض للحال إلى المحل والمحل إلى الحال بعد الحلول ، كما تفرض للرأس ولدى الرأس والتحقيق ههنا أن وجود الإضافة الحقيقية لا يكون إلا فى العقل ولا يكون فى الخارج الا كون الموجود بحيث يحدث في العقل من تصوره الإضافة ، فإن ولادة شخص من شخص أمر موجود في الخارج ، وإذا تصوره العاقل يعقل أبوة في أحدهما وبنوة في الآخر ولا يلزم التسلسل ، لأن الأبوة إذا عرضت لشخص وإن كان ذلك المروض إضافة أخرى لكنها لا تكون بأبوة أخرى فإذا لا تتسلسل الأبوة وتلك الإضافة أيضاً أمر عقلى ولا تتسلسل، لأنها تنقطع عند وقوف العقل ، وهم يقولون إن لله تعالى صفات إضافية كالأول والآخر والخالق والرازق والمبدع والصانع وغير ذلك ، ويلتزمون القول بهذه الصفات غير المعية الزمانية لله تعالى وأما قوله : حصول الوجود الماهية إضافة بينهما فليس بشيء لأن الإضافة مهنا ليست إلا بمعنى الانضمام وليس ذلك ما نحن فيه ، وكون الشىء فى الزمان نسبية كون الجسم في المكان الذى يقول بوجوده المتكلم ، وأما النسبة فيلحقها بعد ثبوتها، وأما التأثير فليس كل تأثير من هذه المقولة بل يريدون التأثير الصادر عن المؤثر في زمان غير قار الذات كقطع السكين اللحم ، فإن الجزءين لا يقعان في زمان واحد ، فالهيئة الحاصلة للسكين حين يقال له هوذا يقطع لا قبله ولا بعده هي المعنية بأن يفعل ، وقس عليه الانفعال. والنسبة إنما تعرض للعقل بين القاطع والمقطوع والإنصاف يقتضى أن ينقل مذاهب الخصوم على ماذهبوا لئلا يلحق الناقلين شناعة بسبب سوء العقل
As for the philosophers, they adduced proof for the existence of these relations: that the heaven being above the earth, for example, is a matter obtained whether assumption and consideration exist or not, and it is not a matter of non-existence, because a thing may not be above and then becomes above. So the 'aboveness' (fawqiyya) that occurs after non-existence cannot be non-existential, otherwise the negation of negation would be non-existential, which is impossible. So 'aboveness' is a positive thing and not... I say: If these categories were relations, they would be species of a higher genus which is relation, and would not be highest genera. They (the philosophers) do not mean by them that relation enters into their essence, but rather that relation accidentally attaches to them, except for 'idafa', for its concept is relation and it requires the determination of relation. As for 'idafa' being an accident inhering in a substrate, its inherence in that substrate is not a relation; rather, relation is supposed between the state and the substrate, and between the substrate and the state after inherence, just as it is supposed between the head and what is at the head. The verification here is that the existence of a real relation is only in the mind, and in the external world there is only the existent being such that from its conception in the mind a relation arises. For example, the birth of a person from a person is a matter existing externally. When the rational person conceives it, he conceives paternity in one and sonship in the other, and infinite regress does not follow, because when paternity attaches to a person, even if that subject of relation is another relation, it is not by another paternity; thus paternity does not regress infinitely, and that relation is a mental thing, and it does not regress because it stops at the mind's stop. They (the philosophers) say that God has relational attributes like 'the First', 'the Last', 'the Creator', 'the Provider', 'the Originator', 'the Maker', and others, and they affirm these attributes, except for temporal 'withness' (ma'iyya) for God. As for his statement: 'the obtaining of existence to quiddity is a relation between them', it is nothing, because 'relation' here is only in the sense of conjunction (indimam), not what we are discussing. And a thing's being in time is relative like a body's being in place, as the mutakallim says exists. As for relation, it attaches to it after its existence. As for influence (ta'thir), not every influence is of this category; rather, they mean the influence emanating from the influencer in non-stationary time, like the cutting of meat by a knife, for the two parts do not occur in one time. So the state acquired by the knife when it is said 'it is cutting'—neither before nor after—is what is meant by 'to act'. Measure upon it passion (infi'al). Relation only occurs in the mind between the cutter and the cut. Fairness requires that the doctrines of the opponents be transmitted as they held, so that transmitters not be disgraced due to poor understanding.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: first, the philosophers' proof for the reality of relational accidents, and second, the editor's extensive clarification and refutation of the mutakallimun's arguments. The philosophers argue that 'aboveness' (fawqiyya) is a real attribute because it can come into existence after not existing; otherwise, the negation of negation would be impossible. The editor then intervenes to explain that the categories of relation are not themselves relations in their essence, except for 'idafa' which is purely relational. He clarifies that inherence of an accident in a substrate is not itself a relation; rather, relation is a mental consideration after inherence. Real relation exists only in the mind, not externally; external things only give rise to mental relations. The example of paternity and sonship illustrates that no infinite regress occurs because these are mental relations that stop at the mind. He also addresses specific objections: God's relational attributes (like Creator) are affirmed; the relation between existence and quiddity is mere conjunction, not the relational category; temporal location is analogous to spatial location; and influence (ta'thir) is a special case of non-stationary action. He concludes with a methodological note about faithfully presenting opponents' views.

Key Terms

النسبة العقلية
الأبوة
البنوة
التأثير
الانفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers believe that relations are physical accidents existing in the outside world.
Correction: The editor insists that real relations exist only in the mind, though they are based on external realities.

Checkpoint
According to the editor, why does the example of paternity not lead to infinite regress?
A) Because paternity is not a real attribute.
B) Because the subject of paternity is not itself a paternity, and the relation stops at the mind.
C) Because infinite regress is impossible in principle.
D) Because God prevents it.

Key Takeaway
The editor establishes that real relations are mental constructs, avoiding infinite regress and resolving apparent conflicts about God's attributes and temporal action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this passage challenge you to purify your concept of Allah?

Think of a time you criticized someone else's belief without truly understanding it. How can you commit to representing others' views accurately, especially in matters of faith, even when you disagree?
?
The verification that real relation only exists in the mind forces you to examine the nature of your own thoughts. When you think of your relationship with Allah—as His slave, His beloved, or His servant—are you aware that these are mental constructs arising from the external reality of His lordship? How does this awareness deepen your gratitude and humility?

Conceptual vs. Fictive Relations in Kalam
Commentator distinguishes conceptual and fictive relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between conceptual (ʿaqli) and fictive (fardī) relations and why confusing them leads to error.

Core Idea
Conceptual relations are necessary in the mind; fictive relations are arbitrary assumptions.

This passage from the commentator on the Muhassal clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction between two kinds of mental relations. Without this distinction, arguments about attributes and relations can go astray. We will unpack what makes a relation 'conceptual' versus 'fictive' and see how the commentator uses the example of the sky's above-ness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the example of the sky's above-ness and below-ness.
The commentator uses a concrete example to illustrate an abstract distinction.
2
Identify the two types of relations
Find how the commentator defines conceptual (ʿaqli) and fictive (fardī). Conceptual relations 'must occur in the intellect when it conceives the thing'; fictive relations are 'what the assumer assumes, even if impossible.'
3
Understand the warning
The commentator stresses that both are mental but must be distinguished to avoid error. Reflect on why confusing them could lead to mistakes in arguments.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : كون الشيء عقليا كفوقية السماء يباين كونه فرضيا، فإن تحتية السماء ربما يفرض ، بل العقلي الذى يجب أن يحدث فى العقل إذا عقل العقل ذلك الشيء كفوقية السماء . وأما الفرضي فهو الذي يفرضه الفارض وإن كان محالا والذهن يشتملهما ، ويجب أن يفهم كل واحد منهما لئلا يقع بسبب الاشتباه غلط .
I say: A thing's being conceptual (ʿaqliyyā), like the sky's being above, is different from its being fictive (fardiyyā). For the sky's being below can sometimes be fictively assumed. Rather, the conceptual is what must occur in the intellect when the intellect conceives that thing, like the sky's above-ness. As for the fictive, it is what the assumer assumes, even if it is impossible. The mind encompasses both, and each must be understood so that error does not occur due to confusion.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This short commentary clarifies a distinction between two types of relative concepts: conceptual (ʿaqli) and fictive (fardī). The conceptual relation, like the sky's above-ness, is a necessary mental consequence when the object is correctly conceived; it is not a mere assumption. The fictive relation is something the mind can arbitrarily assume, even if it is impossible (e.g., the sky being below). Both are mental but differ in grounding. The commentator warns that confusing them leads to mistakes. This distinction supports the philosophers' claim that relative attributes are real (conceptual) rather than merely assumed.

Key Terms
عقلي
فرضي
فوقية
تحتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that conceptual relations are arbitrary or imaginary.
Correction: Conceptual relations are necessary mental outcomes when the object is correctly conceived; they are not arbitrary like fictive assumptions.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between a conceptual (ʿaqli) relation and a fictive (fardī) relation according to the commentator?
A) Conceptual relations exist in external reality; fictive relations are imaginary.
B) Conceptual relations are necessary mental outcomes of conception; fictive relations are arbitrary assumptions.
C) Conceptual relations are eternal; fictive relations are temporal.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
The commentator's distinction prevents errors by grounding relative attributes in necessary mental concepts rather than arbitrary assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In your daily reasoning, are you careful to distinguish between what the mind must conclude from true premises and what you simply choose to assume? Write down one area where you might have confused the two and resolve to correct it.

Infinity and the Concept of Half
Commentator explains that infinite sets can have halves without becoming finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the infinite does not become finite by subtraction or comparison, and that a thing can be finite in one respect and infinite in another, as illustrated by the example of multiplying numbers infinitely.

Core Idea
Infinite sets can have halves without becoming finite.

This lesson clarifies a common objection about infinity and halves. The commentator explains that the infinite remains infinite even when parts are removed or compared. He introduces the idea that something can be finite in one aspect and infinite in another, using the multiplication of a thousand and two thousand an infinite number of times to show that one infinite can be half of another without becoming finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the commentator uses a very compact style with a conditional statement.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the first sentence: 'The infinite does not become finite by the subtraction of everything from it.' This is the key principle.
This directly contradicts the assumption that subtracting from an infinite makes it finite.
3
Understand the qualification
The commentator then qualifies: something can be finite in one respect and infinite in another. Look for the phrase 'ربما يكون متناهيا من وجه غير من وجه'.
This introduces the idea of multiple respects or aspects.
4
Analyze the example
The multiplication of a thousand and two thousand an infinite number of times is given. One infinite series is half the other in terms of the starting number, but both are infinite in length.
Example: infinite series of 1000 and infinite series of 2000.
5
Reflect on the implication
Conclude that 'less than' does not imply finitude for infinite sets. This supports the counter-argument against the objection about halves.
Think about how this relates to the broader discussion of infinity in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : غير المتناهى لا يصير متناهيا بنقصان كل شيء منه ، والتي ربما يكون متناهيا من وجه غير من وجه فلحقه خواص المتناهى من الوجه الأول وخواص غير المتناهى من الوجه الآخر ، وهذا كتضعيف الألف والألفين مرارا لانهاية لها فيكون أحد غير المتناهيين نصف الآخر ، ولا يلزمه منه تناهى أحدهما.
I say: The infinite does not become finite by the subtraction of everything from it. And sometimes a thing may be finite in one respect and not in another, so it acquires the properties of the finite in the first respect and the properties of the infinite in the other respect. This is like the multiplication of a thousand and two thousand an infinite number of times, so one of the two infinities is half of the other, and finitude of either does not follow from that.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This commentary responds to the objection about halves and infinity. The commentator clarifies a key point: subtracting something from an infinite set does not make it finite; it remains infinite. Moreover, a set can be finite in one respect (e.g., in number of units) but infinite in another (e.g., in repetition). He uses the example of infinitely repeating 1000 vs. infinitely repeating 2000: one is half the other in terms of the multiplier (1000 vs 2000), yet both series are infinite in length. Thus, one infinite can be half of another without either becoming finite. This directly supports Ma'mar's counter-argument that 'less than' does not entail finitude.

Key Terms
غير المتناهي
النصف
التضعيف
الخواص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If one quantity is less than another, it must be finite.
Correction: This principle only holds for finite quantities. Infinite sets can be compared such that one is half of the other, yet both remain infinite.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the infinite multiplication of 1000 and 2000?
A) Both become finite because they are multiplied.
B) One is half of the other, but both remain infinite.
C) They are equal in magnitude.
D) They are impossible.

Key Takeaway
Infinite sets can be compared in terms of 'half' without either becoming finite, because infinity is not a quantity that becomes finite by reduction or division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel that your repeated sins 'subtract' from Allah's mercy, do you believe that His mercy becomes finite? How does this lesson's principle—that the infinite does not become finite by subtraction—correct that assumption? What would change in your heart if you truly internalized that Allah's mercy remains infinite regardless of your faults?
?
Consider one area of your life where you act as if Allah's knowledge is finite—perhaps by hiding thoughts or sins, thinking He doesn't know. Since the infinite does not become finite by subtraction, what does that imply about the comprehensiveness of His knowledge? How does this truth call you to live with more sincerity and transparency before Him?

The Argument Against the Reality of Surface, Line, and Point
Razi presents the argument against the reality of continuous entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that surface, line, and point are not real accidents but mere boundaries of bodies.

Core Idea
Surface is just where a body ends, not a thing itself.

This lesson presents a classical argument found in Islamic kalam and philosophy: that continuous entities such as surface, line, and point have no independent existence. The argument proceeds by analyzing what we mean by 'surface'—it is merely the end of a body—and then showing that if it were an accident inhering in a divisible body, it would itself become divisible and thus a body, a contradiction. This challenges the assumption that geometrical objects are real attributes of physical things.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage once. It contains two parts: first, that surface is only the end of a body; second, a reductio argument against surface being an accident.
The footnote is not included in this lesson.
2
Understand the definition
The passage says 'surface is nothing but the end of a body' and 'the end of a thing is where it ends'. This is a definition that reduces surface to a relational concept lacking positive existence.
3
Follow the reductio
If surface were an accident inhering in a body that is divisible in three dimensions (length, width, depth), then what inheres would also be divisible in three dimensions, implying it would itself be a body. That contradicts the assumption that it is an accident.
The key is that an accident inhering in a divisible subject does not necessarily become divisible itself—unless it spreads like heat. The argument assumes it does.
4
Reflect on the implication
If this argument holds, then surface, line, and point are not real entities but merely mental constructs or privations. This sets up the need for a defense.
The next lesson provides that defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحكميات المتصلة فقيل لا معنى للسطح إلا نهاية الجسم ، ونهاية الشيء هي أن يفى ذلك الشيء ، وهذا لا يكون أمرا وجوديا وكذا القول في النقطة والخط وأيضا السطح ولو كان عرضا حالا في الجسم المنقسم في الجهات الثلاث ، والحال في الشيء الذى يكون كذلك ينقسم فى الجهات الثلاث فيكون جسما هذا خلف (۲)
(2) أقول : السطح ليس هو فناء الجسم فقط ، فإن الفناء لا يقبل الإشارة الحسية والسطح يقبلها والتحقيق يقتضى أن هناك ثلاثة أمور فناء للجسم في جهة معينة من جهاته ، ومقدار ذو طول وعرض فقط ، وإضافة تعرض للفناء فيقال له بحسب تلك النهاية جسم ذى نهاية والمقدار موجود بسببه يقبل الإشارة ، والفناء ليس بعدم محض بل عدم أحد أبعاد الجسم وهو مخنه ، والإضافة عارضة لها متأخرة عنها ، وربما يعتبر السطح وحده فى حيث هو مقدار ، وذلك موضوع لعلم الهندسة وكذلك الخط والنقطة ولا يلزم من حلول المسطح فى الجسم انقسامه فى الجهات الثلاث كانقسام الجسم ، لأن ذلك يكون حكم المرض السارى في محله ، وليس السطح ولا الخط ولا النقطة من الأعراض السارية في محلها وكذلك الوحدة والوضع وغير ذلك مما لا ينقسم بانقسام المحل ، فهذا هو تقريرهم في هذا الموضع
As for the continuous entities (al-hakamiyyat al-muttasila): it is said that there is no meaning of surface except the end of a body, and the end of a thing is where that thing ends. This is not an existential matter. The same applies to the point and the line. Also, if surface were an accident inhering in a body that is divisible in three dimensions, then what inheres in such a thing would itself be divisible in three dimensions and would be a body—this is a contradiction. (2) (2) I say: The surface is not merely the termination (fana') of the body, because termination does not admit sensory indication while the surface does. Investigation requires that there are three things: the termination of the body in a specific direction, a magnitude having only length and width, and a relation that attaches to the termination such that it is said, in respect of that termination, to be a body with an end. The magnitude exists by virtue of it and admits sensory indication. The termination is not pure non-existence but rather the absence of one dimension of the body, namely its depth. The relation is accidental and posterior to it. The surface may be considered alone as a magnitude, and that is the subject of geometry. The same applies to the line and the point. The inherence of the planar (musattah) in the body does not entail its division in three dimensions like the division of the body, because that would be the ruling of a spreading accident in its locus. The surface, line, and point are not from the accidents that spread in their locus—likewise unity, position, and others that do not divide with the division of the locus. This is their exposition in this place.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage discusses the reality of continuous entities: surface, line, and point. First, an argument against their existence is given: surface is merely the end of a body and thus not a real existent. If it were an accident inhering in a body that is divisible in three dimensions, then what inheres would also be divisible in three dimensions, becoming a body—a contradiction. The marginal note defends the reality of surface, line, and point as having magnitude and being the subject of geometry. It distinguishes between the termination (fana') which is a privation of a dimension, and the magnitude which is positive and admits sensory indication. The inherence of a surface in a body does not cause it to divide in three dimensions because it is not a spreading accident like heat; rather, it inheres in a single locus considered as a whole. Unity, position, and such are similar.

Key Terms
الحكميات المتصلة
السطح
الخط
النقطة
الفناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think surface is a real accident inhering in a body like color.
Correction: This argument shows that if surface were such an accident, it would lead to a contradiction; thus surface must be something else (or nothing at all).

Checkpoint
What does the argument claim about the definition of surface?
A) Surface is a real accident inhering in a body.
B) Surface is nothing but the end of a body.
C) Surface is a substance.
D) Surface is an imaginary concept.

Key Takeaway
The argument reduces surface to a mere boundary, denying it positive existence, and then shows that treating it as an accident leads to a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often define yourself by your limits—your patience, your knowledge, your ability. But this lesson says the end of a thing is not a thing itself. What if your limits are not real attributes but merely where something in you ceases? How would you live differently if you stopped treating your boundaries as real entities?
?
The argument shows that treating a boundary as a substance leads to contradiction. Think of a time you treated a 'limit' as an unchangeable fact about yourself, like 'I am not a patient person.' Could that limit itself be an illusion? What would it take to see it as merely the end of a pattern, not a fixed trait?
?
If you truly internalize that surface is not a real thing, then the boundaries you perceive—between your knowledge and ignorance, between your strength and weakness—are not real things either. What would you dare to pursue if you saw those boundaries as nothing but the place where your current state ends, not a wall?
