Defending the Reality of Surface as Magnitude
Razi's marginal note defends the reality of surface as magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the marginal note defends the reality of surface as a magnitude with length and width, distinct from mere termination.

Core Idea
Surface is a real magnitude, not just an absence.

The marginal note in Razi's text responds to the earlier argument by distinguishing three aspects of a surface: the termination (fana') of the body, the magnitude that has only length and width, and the relation between them. It argues that termination is not pure non-existence but rather the absence of one dimension (depth). The magnitude is positive and admits sensory indication, forming the subject of geometry. Moreover, surface does not divide with the division of the body because it is not a spreading accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the marginal note
Read the Arabic footnote starting with 'أقول'. This is the author's or commentator's rebuttal.
2
Identify the three things
The note lists: 1) the termination (fana) in a specific direction, 2) a magnitude with only length and width, and 3) a relation attaching to the termination.
These three aspects together explain the concept of surface.
3
Understand the nature of termination
Termination is not pure non-existence but the absence of one dimension (depth). So it is a privation, not an absolute nothing.
4
Why surface does not divide like a body
When a surface inheres in a body, it does not divide in three dimensions because it is not a spreading accident (arad sariy). Like unity and position, it inheres in the whole without partition.
This refutes the earlier reductio.
5
Connect to geometry
The note emphasizes that the magnitude considered alone is the subject of geometry, making surface, line, and point foundational for mathematics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحكميات المتصلة فقيل لا معنى للسطح إلا نهاية الجسم ، ونهاية الشيء هي أن يفى ذلك الشيء ، وهذا لا يكون أمرا وجوديا وكذا القول في النقطة والخط وأيضا السطح ولو كان عرضا حالا في الجسم المنقسم في الجهات الثلاث ، والحال في الشيء الذى يكون كذلك ينقسم فى الجهات الثلاث فيكون جسما هذا خلف (۲)
(2) أقول : السطح ليس هو فناء الجسم فقط ، فإن الفناء لا يقبل الإشارة الحسية والسطح يقبلها والتحقيق يقتضى أن هناك ثلاثة أمور فناء للجسم في جهة معينة من جهاته ، ومقدار ذو طول وعرض فقط ، وإضافة تعرض للفناء فيقال له بحسب تلك النهاية جسم ذى نهاية والمقدار موجود بسببه يقبل الإشارة ، والفناء ليس بعدم محض بل عدم أحد أبعاد الجسم وهو مخنه ، والإضافة عارضة لها متأخرة عنها ، وربما يعتبر السطح وحده فى حيث هو مقدار ، وذلك موضوع لعلم الهندسة وكذلك الخط والنقطة ولا يلزم من حلول المسطح فى الجسم انقسامه فى الجهات الثلاث كانقسام الجسم ، لأن ذلك يكون حكم المرض السارى في محله ، وليس السطح ولا الخط ولا النقطة من الأعراض السارية في محلها وكذلك الوحدة والوضع وغير ذلك مما لا ينقسم بانقسام المحل ، فهذا هو تقريرهم في هذا الموضع
As for the continuous entities (al-hakamiyyat al-muttasila): it is said that there is no meaning of surface except the end of a body, and the end of a thing is where that thing ends. This is not an existential matter. The same applies to the point and the line. Also, if surface were an accident inhering in a body that is divisible in three dimensions, then what inheres in such a thing would itself be divisible in three dimensions and would be a body—this is a contradiction. (2) (2) I say: The surface is not merely the termination (fana') of the body, because termination does not admit sensory indication while the surface does. Investigation requires that there are three things: the termination of the body in a specific direction, a magnitude having only length and width, and a relation that attaches to the termination such that it is said, in respect of that termination, to be a body with an end. The magnitude exists by virtue of it and admits sensory indication. The termination is not pure non-existence but rather the absence of one dimension of the body, namely its depth. The relation is accidental and posterior to it. The surface may be considered alone as a magnitude, and that is the subject of geometry. The same applies to the line and the point. The inherence of the planar (musattah) in the body does not entail its division in three dimensions like the division of the body, because that would be the ruling of a spreading accident in its locus. The surface, line, and point are not from the accidents that spread in their locus—likewise unity, position, and others that do not divide with the division of the locus. This is their exposition in this place.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage discusses the reality of continuous entities: surface, line, and point. First, an argument against their existence is given: surface is merely the end of a body and thus not a real existent. If it were an accident inhering in a body that is divisible in three dimensions, then what inheres would also be divisible in three dimensions, becoming a body—a contradiction. The marginal note defends the reality of surface, line, and point as having magnitude and being the subject of geometry. It distinguishes between the termination (fana') which is a privation of a dimension, and the magnitude which is positive and admits sensory indication. The inherence of a surface in a body does not cause it to divide in three dimensions because it is not a spreading accident like heat; rather, it inheres in a single locus considered as a whole. Unity, position, and such are similar.

Key Terms
الفناء
المقدار
الإضافة
العرض الساري
المسطح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the earlier argument proves surface is nothing at all.
Correction: The note shows that surface has a positive magnitude aspect and is not mere privation.

Checkpoint
According to the marginal note, why is surface not merely the termination of the body?
A) Because termination is imperceptible but surface is perceptible.
B) Because termination is pure non-existence.
C) Because surface is an accident that spreads.
D) Because termination is a relation.

Key Takeaway
Surface is a real, perceptible magnitude with length and width, distinct from the privation of depth, and it does not divide with the body because it is a non-spreading accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the surface of your prayer mat or the pages of the Quran, do you perceive a mere absence of depth, or a real extension that carries meaning? How does affirming the reality of surface affect your trust in the physical world as a sign of Allah's creation?
?
Razi argues that some accidents do not spread or divide with their locus. Are there aspects of your faith—like your intention (niyya) or your love for Allah—that are 'non-spreading' but present in every part of your life? How can you cultivate such an all-encompassing yet indivisible state?
?
If you were pressed to defend the reality of a surface to someone who denies it, would you be able to? What does your answer reveal about your own certainty regarding the knowledge gained from geometry and the physical order established by Allah?

Time: Definition, Objections, and Defense
Razi defines time, gives two objections, and a marginal note refutes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical theological arguments against the real existence of time and the counterargument that avoids infinite regress.

Core Idea
Time's existence was attacked as causing absurdity or regress, but intrinsic priority offers a way out.

This lesson delves into a classic theological debate about time. Razi first reports Aristotle's definition, then presents two rational objections to time being a real entity. Finally, a marginal note provides a defense by showing that the priority of time's parts is intrinsic, not requiring another time. Study carefully to see how early theologians reasoned about fundamental concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its full English translation. Notice the structure: definition, two arguments, and a marginal response.
The marginal note is marked with (3) and begins with 'I say'.
2
Identify the two arguments against time
The passage gives two hypothetical cases: if time were permanent in essence, or if it were non-permanent. Work through each and see why each is presented as a reductio ad absurdum.
The first leads to the present being identical to the past; the second leads to time within time and infinite regress.
3
Analyze the marginal defense
Read the marginal note carefully. How does it respond to each argument? Focus on the concepts of permanent essence and intrinsic priority.
The note denies the claimed absurdities and argues that priority of parts is by essence, not by another time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الزمان فهو مقدار الحركة عند أرسطاطاليس. فقد احتجوا على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون موجودا بأمور. أولها أنه لو كان موجودا امكان إما أن يكون قار الذات فيكون الحاضر عين الماضى فيكون الحادث اليوم حادثا زمان الطوفان هذا خلب، أو لا يكون قار الذات وحينئذ يقضى العقل بأن جزءا منه كان موجودا ولم يبق الآن فإن جزءا منه حصل الآن ، والماضى والآن هو الزمان فيلزم منه وقوع الزمان في الزمان ، فلو كان أمرا وجوديا لزم التسلسل وهو محال (۳)
(3) أقول : إن كان الزمان قار الذات لا يكون الحاضر عين الماضى بل يكون معا في الجسم الذي هو قار الذات ولا يلزم منه أن يكون جزء منه هو عين الجزء الآخر ، وأما إذا كان الزمان غير قار الذات ولم يبق جزء منه عند حصول جزء آخر فلا يلزم منه أن يكون للزمان زمان لأن القبلية والبعدية لأجزاء الزمان لذاتها فيكون جزء مقدما على جزء لا يزمان غيرهما بل بذاتيهما ولا يلزم منه التسلسل .
As for time (zaman), it is the measure of motion according to Aristotle. They have argued that it is not permissible for it to be existent by several points. First: if it were existent, it would either be permanent in essence, in which case the present would be identical to the past, so the event occurring today would be an event at the time of the flood—this is a contradiction; or it is not permanent in essence, and in that case reason judges that a part of it existed and does not remain now, and a part of it occurs now, while the past and the now are time, so it would entail time occurring in time. If it were an existent thing, an infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. (3) (3) I say: If time were permanent in essence, the present would not be identical to the past; rather they would be together in the body that is permanent in essence, and it would not entail that one part is the same as another part. As for if time is not permanent in essence and no part remains when another part occurs, it does not entail that time has a time, because the priority and posteriority of the parts of time are intrinsic to them; so one part is prior to another not by another time but by their essences, and no infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage discusses time. First, Aristotle's definition is given: time is the measure of motion. Then arguments against its real existence are presented: (i) If time were a permanent entity, past and present would coincide, leading to absurdity (e.g., an event today would be at the flood). (ii) If time were non-permanent (flowing), then parts of time come and go, but then the 'now' and 'past' are both time, so time would be within time, causing an infinite regress. The marginal note defends the reality of time by arguing that if time is permanent, past and present coexist in the same permanent being without being identical; if time is non-permanent, the priority of parts is intrinsic, not requiring another time, so no regress occurs.

Key Terms
الزمان
مقدار الحركة
قار الذات
غير قار الذات
التسلسل
القبلية
البعدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That time necessarily entails an infinite regress of times.
Correction: The marginal note shows that priority and posteriority of time's parts are intrinsic; one part is prior by its essence, not by another time, so no regress occurs.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument given against the existence of time as a real entity?
A) Time is only a mental construct.
B) If time were permanent, the present would be identical to the past.
C) Time cannot be measured.
D) Motion does not require time.

Key Takeaway
The debate over time's existence hinges on the nature of its parts: if treated as permanent or flowing, both lead to problems that are resolved by considering intrinsic priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is not a real entity in the way you assume, how urgently would you act on what you know is true, knowing that your 'now' is not a fixed point but a relation?
?
The refutation shows that priority between moments is intrinsic, not measured by another time. What priorities in your life have you been treating as dependent on circumstances rather than as intrinsic to your character?
?
The passage argues that the present does not become the past except by an intrinsic order. If your current moment carries the weight of eternity, what have you delayed that you must do now?

Unity and Infinite Regress
Imam Razi responds to the objection that unity requires another unity, avoiding regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that unity is not an accident requiring a prior unity, thereby avoiding infinite regress, and grasp the distinction between a conceptual property and a real attribute.

Core Idea
Unity is the consideration of indivisibility, not an accident.

Imam Razi addresses a classic objection: if unity inheres in a composite subject, it would need another unity to support it, leading to an infinite chain. His response redefines unity as a conceptual indivisibility, not a real accident. This showcases the analytical precision of kalam in tackling paradoxes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening lines where Razi presents the objection: the difficulty of unity subsisting in a subject is like the difficulty of position, and if it requires a prior unity, infinite regress follows.
The objection assumes unity is an accident like other accidents.
2
Examine the response
Razi first clarifies that configuration (hayʾa) only occurs in a whole, not parts. Then he addresses unity directly: it is the consideration of non-division in the composite.
Use the example of ten: ten as ten is indivisible, though its units are divisible.
3
Grasp the regress argument
Razi explains that repeated unities involve different subjects (the thing and the unity itself), so no number arises in one rank. Thus no regress.
Subjects are not repeated in one rank.
4
Apply the insight
Reflect on how this dissolves the regress: unity does not need a prior unity because it is not a quality that inheres; it is a conceptual attribute.
This is a common move in kalam: reclassifying a problematic concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَنا نقول الإشكال في كيفية قيام تلك الوحدة بها كالإشكال في قيام هيئة الوضع بها، فإن كان بسبب وحدة أخرى سابقة لزم التسلسل وكذا القول في الملك. أقول: الهيئة المسماة بالوضع إنما تحصل في الأجزاء بعد صيرورتها جملة واحدة، وكذلك الزاوية والشكل وليس لك حلول العرض الواحد في محال كثيرة، إنما هو حلول عرض واحد في محل واحد ينقسم باعتبار غير اعتبار وحدته ولم يدل على استحالة ذلك دليلي، وأما الوحدة فهي التي تجعل المجموع واحدا، وإذا اعتبر فيه عدم الانقسام بوجه ما مثلا كمشرة فإنها لا تنقسم من حيث هي عشرة، وإن انقسمت من حيث هي آحاد هي أجزاء العشرة، وقد تتكرر الوحدة حين يقال وحدة واحدة، ولا يلزم منه ثبوته، فإن موضع الوحدة الأولى هو الشيء الذي يقال إنه واحد، وموضوع الوحدة الثانية هو الوحدة الأولى، وإذا لم تتكرر الموضوعات في مرتبة واحدة لم يحصل من الوحدات عدد، وليس قيام الوحدة بالموضوع المنقسم محتاجا إلى وحدة تسبقها، بل هي اعتبار عدم الانقسام فيها من حيث اعتبار كونها ذلك المجموع ولا يلزم التسلسل.
We say: The difficulty in how that unity subsists in it is like the difficulty in how the configuration (hayʾa) of position (waḍʿ) subsists in it. For if it is due to a prior unity, then infinite regress follows, and likewise the statement about [؟]. I say: The configuration called 'position' only occurs in the parts after they become one whole, likewise the angle and shape. And you do not have the indwelling of one accident in many loci; rather it is the indwelling of one accident in one locus that is divisible by a consideration other than its unity. No proof has indicated the impossibility of that. As for unity, it is that which makes the composite one. And if non-division in some respect is considered in it, for example ten: it does not divide inasmuch as it is ten, though it divides in terms of the units which are parts of ten. Unity may be repeated when one says 'one unity', but that does not necessitate its subsistence. For the subject of the first unity is the thing said to be one, and the subject of the second unity is the first unity. If subjects are not repeated in one rank, no number arises from unities. And the subsistence of unity in the divisible subject does not need a prior unity; rather it is the consideration of non-division in it from the perspective of its being that composite, and no infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objection claims that unity in a composite subject leads to infinite regress because if unity subsists in a divisible subject, it would require another unity to account for its subsistence, and so on. The response clarifies that unity is not an accident inhering in a divisible subject as other accidents do. Rather, it is the consideration of non-division in the subject relative to being that composite. For example, ten is not divisible as ten, even though its units are divisible. The subject of unity is the whole, not the parts. Therefore, no regress occurs because unity is not a quality that itself requires a further unity; it is merely the conceptual indivisibility of the composite.

Key Terms
الوحدة (al-waḥda): unity
الوضع (al-waḍʿ): position
التسلسل (al-tasalsul): infinite regress
العرض (al-ʿaraḍ): accident

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think unity is a quality that inheres in a subject like an accident.
Correction: Razi explains that unity is not an accident; it is a conceptual consideration of non-division in the composite, so it does not require a prior unity.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's response, what is unity (al-waḥda) primarily considered as?
A) An accident inhering in a divisible subject
B) The consideration of non-division in a composite subject
C) A separate substance that prevents division
D) The number one

Key Takeaway
Unity is not an accident that requires prior unity; it is the conceptual indivisibility of a composite, so infinite regress does not apply.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'Allah is one' — but have you ever paused to ask yourself: are you thinking of that oneness as an accidental quality inhering in His essence, or as a conceptual indivisibility beyond comparison? How would this distinction affect your certainty in tawhid?
?
This lesson resolves an infinite regress by distinguishing between subjects at different levels. What is one thought pattern or worry in your life that spirals into endless 'what ifs'? Can you identify a 'subject' that is in a higher rank — something foundational — that could stop the regress?
?
If unity is merely a consideration of non-division, what does that mean for how you approach understanding divine attributes? Are you comfortable with a concept of oneness that is not a 'thing' but a way of seeing? What does that demand from your intellect and your heart?

The Reality of Surface: Quantity, Not Mere Privation
Imam Razi refutes the claim that surface is merely the end of a body and non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that surface is a real quantity (miqdār) with length and width, not merely the privation of a body.

Core Idea
Surface is a real quantity, not just an end or privation.

Imam Razi confronts a claim that surface is merely the end of a body and thus non-existent. He refutes this by analyzing surface into three components: privation of depth, a quantity with length and width, and a relational accident. This establishes surface as a genuine existent and the subject of geometry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the initial claim that surface is nothing but the end of a body, hence non-existent, and the objection about divisibility.
The objection uses the term 'fanā'' (privation).
2
Identify Imam Razi's three-part analysis
After refuting the claim, Razi explains that surface involves three things: a privation of depth, a quantity (miqdār) with length and width, and an accidental relation. Locate these in the text.
The quantity is what makes surface perceptible.
3
Understand why surface is not a pervasive accident
Razi differentiates surface from pervasive accidents that divide with their locus. Surface is a non-pervasive accident like unity or position, so it does not become a body.
This addresses the second part of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحكميات المتصلة فقيل لا معنى للسطح إلا نهاية الجسم، ونهاية الشيء هي أن يفنى ذلك الشيء، وهذا لا يكون أمرا وجوديا وكذا القول في النقطة والخط. وأيضا السطح ولو كان عرضا حالا في الجسم المنقسم في الجهات الثلاث، والحال في الشيء الذي يكون كذلك ينقسم في الجهات الثلاث فيكون جسما هذا خلف. أقول: السطح ليس هو فناء الجسم فقط، فإن الفناء لا يقبل الإشارة الحسية والسطح يقبلها والتحقيق يقتضي أن هناك ثلاثة أمور: فناء للجسم في جهة معينة من جهاته، ومقدار ذو طول وعرض فقط، وإضافة تعرض للفناء فيقال له بحسب تلك النهاية جسم ذي نهاية والمقدار موجود بسببه يقبل الإشارة، والفناء ليس بعدم محض بل عدم أحد أبعاد الجسم وهو عمقه، والإضافة عارضة لها متأخرة عنها، وربما يعتبر السطح وحده في حيث هو مقدار، وذلك موضوع لعلم الهندسة وكذلك الخط والنقطة ولا يلزم من حلول المسطح في الجسم انقسامه في الجهات الثلاث كانقسام الجسم، لأن ذلك يكون حكم المرض الساري في محله، وليس السطح ولا الخط ولا النقطة من الأعراض السارية في محلها وكذلك الوحدة والوضع وغير ذلك مما لا ينقسم بانقسام المحل، فهذا هو تقريرهم في هذا الموضع.
As for the connected judgments [related to continuous quantities], it is said: There is no meaning for surface except the end of a body, and the end of a thing is that which annihilates that thing, and this is not an existent thing; likewise the statement regarding the point and the line. Also, if surface were an accident inhering in the body which is divisible in the three dimensions, then what inheres in a thing that is such [i.e., divisible in three dimensions] would itself be divisible in the three dimensions, thus becoming a body—this is a contradiction. I say: Surface is not merely the privation of the body, for privation does not admit of sensory indication, but surface does admit it. Investigation requires that there are three things: a privation of the body in a specific direction among its directions, a quantity (miqdār) having length and width only, and an accident (iḍāfa) that attaches to the privation, so that it is called, by virtue of that end, a finite body. The quantity exists by means of it and admits sensory indication. Privation is not pure non-existence, but the non-existence of one dimension of the body, namely its depth. The accident is incidental to it, posterior to it. Sometimes surface is considered alone inasmuch as it is a quantity, and that is the subject matter of geometry; likewise the line and the point. It does not follow from the indwelling of a planar surface in the body that it is divisible in the three dimensions as the body is divisible, because that would be the rule of a pervasive accident in its locus. And neither surface, nor line, nor point are among the pervasive accidents in their locus; likewise unity, position, and other things that do not divide with the division of the locus. This is their formulation in this place.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and clarification. The objection claims that a surface is merely the end of a body, and an end is a privation, hence non-existent; also if it were an accident, it would be divisible like the body, leading to contradiction. The response demonstrates that surface has a real quantity (miqdār) with length and width, existing in the body, but it is not a pervasive accident that divides with the body. Rather, it is a non-pervasive accident, like unity, that does not divide. Thus, surface is a genuine existent and the subject of geometry.

Key Terms
السطح (al-saṭḥ): surface
الفناء (al-fanāʾ): privation/annihilation
المقدار (al-miqdār): quantity
العرض الساري (al-ʿaraḍ al-sārī): pervasive accident

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if surface inheres in a divisible body, it must also be divisible.
Correction: Not all accidents are pervasive; surface is a non-pervasive accident like unity, so it does not divide with the body.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi identify as the subject matter of geometry?
A) Body as substance
B) Privation of dimensions
C) Surface, line, and point considered as quantities
D) The pervasive accidents of bodies

Key Takeaway
Surface is a real, non-pervasive accident that constitutes the subject of geometry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now understand that a surface is not a mere nothing but a real quantity. When you look at the world around you, do you tend to dismiss the 'boundaries' and 'surfaces' as insignificant? How might this lesson change your perception of the physical reality Allah has created? Will you start seeing the mathematical order that underlies even the simplest objects as a sign of the Creator's wisdom?
?
Razi argues that even privation (the absence of depth) is not pure non-existence but a real dimension missing. Think of a time you considered something as 'just empty' or 'just a void'—like the space in your heart for worship, or the time you waste. Could those too be real privations that you are accountable for? What would it mean for you to treat them as real absences that need to be filled with substance?
?
The fact that surface is a non-pervasive accident means it does not have to be completely divided with its locus. Are there parts of your faith that you treat as 'pervasive'—thinking a small sin ruins everything? Or conversely, do you dismiss small acts of worship as 'non-pervasive' and thus unimportant? How does the precise theological distinction here invite you to re-evaluate your own spiritual accounting?
